Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is another answer (jawab akhar) to an objection that if duration (mudda) is finite, then God's existence must also be finite. The author refutes this by analogy: the finitude of the world in place (i.e., its spatial boundedness) does not imply that God's essence is finite, because God has no relation (munasaba) to spatial things or space itself. Similarly, the finitude of the world in time does not imply the finitude of God's existence, because God has no relation to temporal things or time. The objection incorrectly assumes a parallelism where none exists. The author then challenges the opponent's other claim: even if duration were infinite in intellectual estimation, it does not follow that actual existents can exist in it in sensory reality, because mental estimation does not guarantee actual existence.

The Argument from Relational Attributes
The author argues that relational attributes do not necessitate multiplicity in the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how relational attributes allow multiplicity in effects without multiplicity in the essence of the Necessary Existent.

Core Idea
Relational attributes do not multiply the essence — they only add relations.

This passage delves into the nature of relational attributes (al-umūr al-iḍāfiyya). It argues that even if the Necessary Existent is related to many things, His essence remains one. The author uses the analogy of the active intellect to illustrate that many forms can emanate from a single source without change. This is crucial for defending divine transcendence against philosophical objections that multiple relations imply multiplicity in God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses a compressed argumentative style.
2
Identify the key claim
Find where the author states that relational attributes do not multiply the essence. This is the core principle of the argument.
3
Examine the analogy
Look at how the author compares the Necessary Existent to the active intellect. Understand the analogy: many forms from one source without change.
4
Understand the conclusion
See how the author applies the analogy to God: all things relate to Him by one relation, and multiplicity arises from recipients.
This reinforces divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : كونه واجب الوجود بغيره أمر إضافي لا محالة ؛ لأن كون واجب الوجود مبدأ وعلة أو موجباً ومبدعًا أمر إضافي لا تتكثر به الذات، فإذا كان الإيجاب إضافة فيكون بين الموجب والموجب إضافة لا محالة، والأمر الإضافي قد يتكثر ويتعدد ولا تتكثر به الذات، فلو صلح أن يكون موجبا في العقل الأول العقل الآخر ) الصح مثله في واجب الوجود بذاته ، فهلا كثرت الإضافات في حق واجب الوجود حتى تتحقق له نسبة إيجاب إلى موجب فتكثر النسب الإضافية ولا تتكثر بها الذات وهذا كما يقدرونه في العقل الفعال الذي هو واهب الصور على العالم الجسماني، فإنه ما من صورة تحدث في العالم إلا وهي من فيضه وسنخه ثم تختلف الصور باختلاف الأنواع والأصناف إلى ما لا يتناهى، ولا تختلف الصفات في الفايض، بل هو على نعت واحد والصور تختلف وتتكثر بحسب القوابل والحوامل، ولو اختلفت النسب، فإنما هي إضافات لا صفات حقيقية، كذلك يجب أن يقال في واجب الوجود بذاته : إنه ينسب إليه الكل نسبة واحدة من حيث وجودها الممكن ثم الاختلاف يحصل في القوابل، أو تكون الإضافات والسلوب كثيرة، وتتعدد الموجبات بحسب تلك، ولا يوجب ذلك كثرة في الذات كما قالوا في المعلول الأول).
Then we say: Its being necessary through another is a relational matter without doubt, because the Necessary Existent's being a principle and cause or an obligator and producer is a relational matter by which the essence does not become multiple. So if causing is a relation, then there is a relation between the causer and the caused without doubt, and the relational matter may multiply and become numerous without the essence becoming multiple thereby. If it is valid that it is a causer in the first intellect [the other intellect] - the correct [view] is [that this is] similar in the Necessary Existent in itself - then why are not the relations multiplied with respect to the Necessary Existent until it has a relation of causation to a caused, so the relational relations multiply without the essence becoming multiple? And this is like what they posit regarding the active intellect which is the bestower of forms upon the corporeal world: there is no form that occurs in the world except that it is from its emanation and its nature, then the forms differ according to the difference of types and species to infinity, and the attributes do not differ in the emanator, rather it is upon one description, and the forms differ and multiply according to the recipients and bearers. And if the relations differ, they are only relations, not real attributes. Likewise it must be said regarding the Necessary Existent in itself: that all things are attributed to Him with one attribution from the aspect of their possible existence, then difference occurs in the recipients, or the relations and negations are many, and the causes of multiplicity become numerous according to those, and that does not necessitate multiplicity in the essence, as they said regarding the first effect.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a further argument against the philosophers. The author points out that the First Intellect's being 'necessary through another' is a relational attribute (amr idafi). Relational attributes do not multiply the essence. The philosophers themselves accept that for the active intellect (al-'aql al-fa''al), many forms emanate from it without its essence becoming multiple. The same should hold for the Necessary Existent: all things are related to Him by a single relation, and any multiplicity arises from the recipients, not from His essence. The author also suggests that even if one allows multiple relational attributes or negations (sulub), they do not necessitate multiplicity in the essence, just as the philosophers say regarding the first effect (the First Intellect).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Relational attributes are real attributes that would multiply the essence.
Correction: Relational attributes are merely relations; they do not affect the essence itself.

Checkpoint

A) A real attribute
B) A relational matter
C) A negative property
D) An essential property

Key Takeaway
Relational attributes allow God to be related to many things while His essence remains absolutely one.

The Problem of Negative Attributes and Multiple Intellects
The author argues that negative attributes do not necessitate multiple substances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the philosophers' argument that negative attributes would necessitate many intellectual substances, and how this objection supports the unity of the Necessary Existent.

Core Idea
Negations do not create realities; many negations do not mean many intellects.

The philosophers claim that the First Intellect is an intellect because it is abstracted from matter—a negative attribute. The author cleverly turns this against them: if negation distinguishes one intellect, then many negations would require many intellects, which is absurd. Moreover, the same negations apply to Allah without producing multiplicity. This lesson unpacks this elegant reductio ad absurdum.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Focus on the logical flow from the initial claim to the conclusion.
Notice the list of negations: matter, form, quality, quantity, position, direction, place, time.
2
Identify the philosophers' claim
The philosophers say the First Intellect is an intellect because it is abstracted from matter (a negation). Understand why they think this makes it a distinct substance.
3
Follow the reductio argument
The author asks: if one negation makes it an intellect, then many negations would make many intellects. List the negations and see why this is absurd.
The negations are enumerated: form, quality, etc.
4
Apply the same logic to Allah
Note that the same negations are realized for the Necessary Existent, but they do not cause multiple substances. This shows the philosophers' reasoning is flawed.
If the philosophers concede this, the author's own purpose is achieved.
5
Grasp the conclusion
The argument fails to prove multiple intellects from negative attributes, and the author's position—that all attributes are attributed to the Necessary Existent alone—remains intact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونقول : كون العقل الأول عقلا أمر سلبي عندكم ؛ لأن معنى كونه عقلا أنه مجرد عن المادة والتجريد عن المادة سلب المادة ونفيها والنفي كيف يناسب وجود جوهر عقلي هو عقل كيف والسلوب كثيرة ؟ فهلا أوجب بكل سلب جوهرًا عقليا، فإنه كما يسلب عنه المادة يسلب عنه الصورة أعني : الصورة الجسمية - ويسلب عنه الكيفية والكمية والوضع والجيز والمكان والزمان، ولم يجب أن يقال : يلزم عنه بكل سلب جوهر عقلي، ومثل هذه السلوب تتحقق في حق واجب الوجود أيضًا، ولا يوجب كل سلب جوهرًا عقليًا، وإن سلم ذلك حصل غرضنا من إضافة الكل إليه.
And we say: That the First Intellect is an intellect is a negative matter according to you, because the meaning of its being an intellect is that it is abstracted from matter, and abstraction from matter is the negation of matter and its denial. How does negation accord with the existence of an intellectual substance that is an intellect? How [can it] when the negations are many? So why does each negation not necessitate an intellectual substance? For just as matter is negated from it, so too are negated from it: form (i.e., bodily form), quality, quantity, position, direction, place, and time. And it is not necessary to say that each negation necessitates an intellectual substance. And similar negations are realized with respect to the Necessary Existent as well, and each negation does not necessitate an intellectual substance. And if that is granted, our purpose is achieved in attributing all things to Him.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage presents another objection based on negative attributes. The author notes that according to the philosophers, the First Intellect being an intellect means it is abstract from matter, which is a negation (salb). But there are many negations (of matter, form, quality, quantity, position, direction, place, time). If each negation necessitated a separate intellectual substance, then there would be many intellects, which is absurd. Moreover, the same negations apply to the Necessary Existent, but they do not produce multiple substances in His case. So the philosophers' argument fails, and the author's point—that all things are attributed to the Necessary Existent—stands.

Key Terms
سلب
تجريد
جوهر عقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that negations (like abstraction from matter) are real properties that constitute positive realities.
Correction: Negations are merely the absence of certain attributes; they are not positive entities that can serve as a basis for distinguishing multiple substances. The passage shows that if negations were real, they would multiply substances ad absurdum.

Checkpoint
What does the author mean by 'negative matter' (أمر سلبي) regarding the First Intellect?
A) It does not exist
B) It is abstracted from matter
C) It has no attributes
D) It is purely potential

Key Takeaway
The argument that negative attributes necessitate multiple intellects is refuted by the fact that many negations would imply many intellects, which is absurd, and the same negations apply to God without causing multiplicity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your understanding of God, do you unconsciously treat His attributes—like His mercy, power, and knowledge—as separate realities, causing a hidden multiplicity in your heart? How can this lesson's emphasis on negations not creating multiplicity transform the way you relate to His oneness in worship?
?
The philosophers argued that abstraction from matter makes the First Intellect a separate substance. What are the 'abstractions' or 'negations' you use to define your own identity? Are you defining yourself by what you are not rather than by what you are in relation to God? How does this passage challenge you to ground your identity in the One Necessary Existent alone?
?
If each negation of the First Intellect could be thought to imply a separate intellect, what are the negations in your life that you have allowed to create divisions in your heart? Are you allowing the absence of something (wealth, status, comfort) to define separate spaces in your soul, instead of directing all your attention to the One who is beyond all negations? What step will you take today to unify your heart's focus?

Evil as Non-Existence in Ash'ari Thought
The author explains the Ash'ari position that evil is merely non-existence or lack of perfection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari definition of evil as non-existence and how it preserves God's transcendence and goodness.

Core Idea
Evil has no reality; it is merely the absence of good.

The Ash'ari theologians faced a deep theological problem: if God is all-good and all-powerful, why does evil exist? In this passage, they offer a sophisticated answer: evil is not a positive entity with its own essence but simply the absence of existence or perfection. This allows them to affirm that God's primary intention is always good, while evil enters only as an accidental byproduct.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Let the author's phrasing and rhythm sink in.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the core definition
Notice the definition of evil (sharr) at the beginning: it is 'non-existence of existence or non-existence of perfection'.
This is the foundation of the argument.
3
Trace the two intentions
Look for the distinction between qaṣd awwal (first intention) and qaṣd thānī (second intention). See how God's primary aim is existence, and evil only enters as a secondary consequence.
This distinction is crucial.
4
Reflect on the defense
Ask yourself: how does this argument protect God from being the direct creator of evil? Does it make sense to say evil is merely absence?
Consider possible objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم جرحوا دلالة الخير والشر بل وقوع الشر في الوجود على مذهبهم؛ بأن قالوا: الشر لا معنى له إلا عدم وجود أو عدم كمال وجود، والعدم يدخل في القضاء بالعرض لا بالذات، بل القصد الأول في الإبداع أن يكون وجود، ثم القسمة العقلية أن يكون وجود هو خير محض، أو يكون وجود لا يتحقق حصوله إلا على أن يتبعه شر، أما وجود الشر المحض فهو مستحيل، بل الوجود الذي أكثره شر كذلك فلا وجود وجود يتبعه شر قليل أكثر شرا من وجوده، فالشر داخل في الوجود بالقصد الثاني، ولا دلالة له على مدلول؛ إذ لا حقيقة لوجوده كما بيناه.
Then they criticized the implication of good and evil, rather the occurrence of evil in existence, according to their doctrine, by saying: Evil has no meaning except non-existence of existence or non-existence of perfection of existence. Non-existence enters into decree accidentally, not essentially. Rather, the first intention in origination is that there be existence. Then the rational division is that existence is either pure good, or an existence that cannot be realized except with evil following it. As for pure evil existence, it is impossible. Rather, even an existence whose majority is evil is likewise; so there is no existence whose following evil is less than its good while its evil is more than its good. Thus evil enters existence by the second intention, and it has no implication for an implication, since its existence has no reality as we have clarified.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the Ash'ari position regarding evil. The author begins by stating that the Ash'aris have refuted the objection that the existence of evil in the world counts against the goodness of God. They define evil (sharr) as nothing but non-existence (ʿadam) of existence or non-existence of perfection of existence. Non-existence, they say, is included in the divine decree accidentally (bil-ʿaraḍ), not essentially (bil-dhāt). God's primary intention (al-qaṣd al-awwal) in creation is existence itself. Then a rational division is proposed: existence is either pure good (khayr maḥḍ) or an existence that cannot be realized except with evil consequent upon it. Pure evil existence is impossible. Even an existence whose majority is evil is likewise impossible; there is no existence whose accompanying evil is less than its good while its evil outweighs its good. Therefore, evil enters existence only as a secondary intention (al-qaṣd al-thānī). It has no implication for any meaning, because its existence has no real positive reality. This argument defends God's wisdom and goodness by denying evil any positive status, thus preserving divine transcendence.

Key Terms
شر
عدم
خير
قصد أول
قصد ثان
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Evil is a positive created entity that God directly wills and creates.
Correction: Ash'aris hold that evil is merely the absence of good, not a positive creation. God only intends existence; evil enters as an accidental byproduct.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari position in this passage, what is evil?
A) A positive created entity with its own essence.
B) Non-existence or lack of perfection of existence.
C) A necessary consequence of free will.
D) The opposite of good, created by God intentionally.

Key Takeaway
Evil is not a positive reality; it is merely non-existence, so God's creation is essentially good and He does not directly will evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face suffering or evil in your life, do you immediately blame God or question His goodness? After this lesson, can you see that what you experience as 'evil' might be a necessary lack in a finite world, rather than a positive act of God? How does that shift your response?
?
If evil has no positive reality but is merely absence of good, what does that say about the evil within your own soul—your sins and shortcomings? Are they real things you must fight, or are they absences of good that you must fill with God's light? Commit to identifying one absence in your character today and seeking the corresponding good.
?
Do you ever find yourself desiring something you think is good, only to realize it brings harm? Reflect on the idea that God's 'first intention' is always good, and any evil is secondary. How can this truth help you trust God's decree even when you don't understand the suffering you see?

Divine Transcendence

Affirming Divine Transcendence
States the foundation and affirms transcendence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth foundation of Sunni creed regarding refuting anthropomorphism and how the People of Truth affirm God's absolute uniqueness.

Core Idea
God does not resemble any creature in any way.

The author begins the fourth foundation by stating its purpose: refuting those who liken God to creation. He immediately clarifies the correct doctrine: Allah is not like anything and nothing is like Him. This lesson presents the foundational negations that protect divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Let the author's rhythm come through.
2
Identify the refuted groups
Notice which groups are mentioned at the start: People of Form, People of Direction, and the Karramiyyah.
They all claimed God is a locus of accidents.
3
List the negations
The People of Truth negate many attributes from God. Find each negation: not a substance, not a body, not an accident, not in place/time, etc.
These negations flow from the Quranic verse cited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة الرابعة في إبطال التشبيه وفيها الرد على أصحاب الصور وأصحاب الجهة والكرامية في قولهم : إن الرب تعالى محل للحوادث. فمذهب أهل الحق أن الله سبحانه لا يشبه شيئاً من المخلوقات ولا يشبهه شيء منها بوجه من وجوه المشابهة والمماثلة ( لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ وَهُوَ السَّمِيعُ الْبَصِيرُ ) [الشورى: ۱۱] فليس الباري سبحانه بجوهر ولا جسم ولا عرض، ولا في مكان ولا في زمان ولا قابل للأعراض، ولا محل للحوادث. وذهبت الغالية من الشيعة إلى نوعي تشبيه: أحدهما تشبيه الخالق بالخلق، فقالت المغيرية والبيانية والهاشمية ومن تابعهم: إنه الإله ذو صورة مثل صور الإنسان، ونسج على منوالهم جماعة من مشبهة الصفاتية متمسكين بقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم: خلق الله آدم على صورة الرحمن، وفي رواية: على صورته. والنوع الثاني تشبيه المخلوق بالخالق، فقالت هؤلاء من الغالية وجماعة أخرى: إن شخصاً من الأشخاص إله أو فيه جزء من الإله، والإله سبحانه وتعالى عن قولهم متشخص به، نسج على منوال النصارى والحلولية في كل أمة. ومن الكرامية من صار إلى أنه جوهر وجسم.
The Fourth Foundation: On Refuting Anthropomorphism. It contains the refutation of the People of Form, the People of Direction, and the Karramiyyah regarding their statement that the Lord, exalted, is a locus of accidents. The doctrine of the People of Truth is that Allah, glorified, does not resemble any created thing nor does any created thing resemble Him in any aspect of similitude or likeness. 'There is nothing like Him, and He is the All-Hearing, the All-Seeing' (Quran 42:11). Thus the Creator, glorified, is not a substance, nor a body, nor an accident, nor in a place, nor in time, nor receptive to accidents, nor a locus of events. The extremists among the Shia went to two types of anthropomorphism: one is likening the Creator to creation—the Mughiriyyah, the Bayaniyyah, the Hashimiyyah, and their followers said that the deity has a form like the form of humans—and a group of anthropomorphists among the Attribute-people followed their pattern, clinging to his statement, peace be upon him: 'God created Adam in the form of the Most Merciful,' and in another narration: 'in His form.' The second type is likening creation to the Creator—these extremists and another group said that a certain person is a god or has a part of divinity within him, and God, glorified and exalted above their saying, is personalized by him, following the pattern of Christians and incarnationists in every nation. And among the Karramiyyah, some held that He is a substance and a body.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage marks the beginning of the Fourth Foundation (القاعدة الرابعة), which is devoted to refuting anthropomorphism (التشبيه). The author states that this foundation includes refutation of several heretical groups: the People of Form (أصحاب الصور), the People of Direction (أصحاب الجهة), and the Karramiyyah, who claim that God is a locus of accidents (محل للحوادث). The correct doctrine (مذهب أهل الحق) is then stated: Allah does not resemble any creature nor does any creature resemble Him in any way. The author cites Quran 42:11 ('There is nothing like Him...') as the scriptural proof. From this, he deduces that God is not a substance (جوهر), not a body (جسم), not an accident (عرض), not in a place or time, not receptive to accidents, and not a locus of events. Next, he discusses deviant sects. The extremist Shia (الغالية من الشيعة) are divided into two types of anthropomorphism. The first type likens the Creator to creation: the Mughiriyyah, Bayaniyyah, Hashimiyyah, and their followers claim that God has a human-like form, and they are joined by some anthropomorphists among the Attribute-people (مشبهة الصفاتية) who cite the hadith 'God created Adam in the form of the Most Merciful' (or 'in His form'). The second type likens creation to the Creator: these extremists and another group claim that a specific person is a god or contains a part of divinity, following the pattern of Christians and incarnationists (الحلولية). Finally, some Karramiyyah hold that God is a substance and a body (جوهر وجسم). All these views are rejected by the People of Truth.

Key Terms
التشبيه
أصحاب الصور
أصحاب الجهة
الكرامية
الجوهر
الجسم
العرض
محل الحوادث
المماثلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the People of Truth, which of the following is NOT true about Allah?
A) He is not a substance or body.
B) He is not in a place or time.
C) He has a form like human form.
D) He is not receptive to accidents.

Key Takeaway
The People of Truth affirm that Allah is absolutely unique, not resembling any creature in any way, which necessitates negating substance, body, accident, place, time, and being a locus for events.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray, do you ever unconsciously imagine God as being 'up there' or in a specific direction? How does this passage challenge that mental image, and what will you do to train yourself to think of God as beyond place?
?
The passage says God is not a 'locus of events.' Does your daily life reflect a belief that God is unchanging and beyond time, or do you sometimes act as if God's actions are like human reactions? What specific habit can you change to align your heart with this truth?
?
If God does not resemble any creature, then your mental image of God—no matter how noble—is ultimately inadequate. Are you willing to surrender that comfortable image and worship a God beyond your comprehension? What is holding you back from doing so?

Deviant Anthropomorphic Sects
Lists deviant sects and their anthropomorphic beliefs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of anthropomorphism that the extremist Shia and others fell into, and how the People of Truth reject them.

Core Idea
Wrong beliefs about God arise from either likening Him to creation or elevating creation to His level.

After establishing the correct doctrine, the author turns to specific sects that deviated. He categorizes the errors of the extremist Shia into two types and also mentions the Karramiyyah's claim. This lesson examines these deviations to protect the believer from them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice the classification into two types of anthropomorphism.
2
Identify the first type
The first type: likening the Creator to creation. Find the sects mentioned and their claim that God has a human-like form. Note the hadith they misuse.
The hadith 'God created Adam in His form' is used by them.
3
Identify the second type
The second type: likening creation to the Creator. Find who claims that a person is a god or contains a part of divinity. Note the comparison to Christians and incarnationists.
4
Note the Karramiyyah deviation
The passage ends with the Karramiyyah claiming that God is a substance and a body. This is another form of anthropomorphism.

Checkpoint
What was the claim of the Mughiriyyah, Bayaniyyah, and Hashimiyyah regarding God?
A) God is not a substance or body.
B) God has a form like the form of humans.
C) God is incarnate in a person.
D) God is a locus of accidents.

Key Takeaway
Deviant sects fall into two types of anthropomorphism: either likening God to creation or elevating creation to God's level, both of which contradict the Quranic principle that nothing is like Him.

?
Have you ever caught yourself imagining God in a form or shape similar to something in creation? What does this lesson reveal about the danger of such mental images, and how can you train your heart to transcend them?
?
This passage shows that entire communities fell into error by misinterpreting a single hadith. Are there any beliefs you hold about God that might be based on weak or literalist interpretations of texts? What concrete step will you take today to seek the understanding of the righteous predecessors?
?
The extremists elevated certain individuals to divine status. Is there anyone in your life—a teacher, leader, or charismatic figure—whom you might be inadvertently revering beyond measure? Commit to examining your heart and redirecting all ultimate reverence to the One who alone deserves it.

Divine Transcendence
beginner

The General Proof: Why Anthropomorphism Implies Contingency
Presents a comprehensive rational argument refuting all anthropomorphic views by linking measure, shape, and change to contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument proving that any attribution of measure, shape, or change to Allah would entail contingency, and therefore He is transcendent beyond all such attributes.

Core Idea
Measure, shape, and change are signs of contingency; God is free of them.

The author begins with a general proof that refutes all forms of anthropomorphism at once. He argues that if God were subject to measure, form, or change, He would be contingent. By examining the rational necessity of a particularizer for any specific determination, this argument establishes God's transcendence beyond any anthropomorphic concept.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the overall flow of the argument.
Notice how the author starts with a comprehensive claim and then unfolds the reasoning.
2
Identify the key link: determination and change → contingency
The author asserts that determination (measure, shape) and change are proofs of contingency. Identify this link in the text.
This is the core premise of the proof.
3
Understand the rational premise about equal measures
The author argues that all measures are equally possible in reason, so any specific measure requires a particularizer (mukhassis). Reflect on why this shows contingency.
If something is possible in multiple ways, its actual specific state needs a cause.
4
Reflect on the example from our own essence
The author uses the example of our own essence acquiring a new attribute, showing that change indicates contingency. Consider how this relates to God.
Change in us proves we are originated; God is not subject to such change.
5
Note the conclusion: God is beyond all anthropomorphic attributes
The argument concludes that God cannot have measure, shape, direction, or originated attributes, as these would imply a determiner or changer, and thus contingency.
This refutes all anthropomorphic views in one stroke.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لنا دليل شامل يعم إبطال مذاهب المشبهة جملة، وعلى كل فرقة ممن عددناهم حجة خاصة ونقض على الانفراد فنبتدي بالأعم، ونقول: التقدير بالأشكال والصور والتغير بالحوادث والتغير دليل الحدوث، فلو كان الباري سبحانه متقدرًا بقدر، متصوراً بصورة متناهيا بحد ونهاية مختصاً بجهة، متغيرا بصفة حادثة في ذاته لكان محدثًا؛ إذ العقل بصريحه يقضي أن الأقدار في تجويز العقل متساوية، فما من قدر وشكل يقدره العقل إلا ويجوز أن يكون مخصوصاً بقدر آخر، واختصاصه بقدر معين وتميزه بجهة ومسافة يستدعي مخصصاً، ومن المعلوم الذي لا مراء فيه أن ذاتنا لم تكن موصوفة بصفة ثم صارت موصوفة فقد تغيرت عما كانت عليه، والتغير دليل الحدوث فإذا لم يستدل على حدوث الكاينات إلا بالتغير الطارئ عليها، وبالجملة فالتغير يستدعي مغيرًا خارجًا من ذات المغير، والمقدر يستدعي مقدرا.
We have a comprehensive proof that generally invalidates the doctrines of the anthropomorphists, and for every sect among those we have enumerated there is a specific argument and refutation individually. So we begin with the general, and we say: Determination by shapes and forms, and change by accidents—and change is a proof of contingency. So if the Creator—glory be to Him—were determined by a measure, formed by a form, finite by a limit and an end, particularized by a direction, changing by an originated attribute in His essence, He would be contingent. For the reason in its clear judgment decrees that measures, in the estimation of reason, are equal. There is no measure or shape that reason can conceive except that it is possible for it to be particularized by another measure. And its particularization by a specific measure and its distinguishing by a direction and distance requires a particularizer. And it is known beyond doubt that our essence was not described by an attribute and then became described—it has changed from what it was. And change is a proof of contingency. For we have not inferred the contingency of beings except by the change that occurs to them. In sum: change requires a changer external to the essence of the changed, and the determined requires a determiner.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage presents a general argument (dail shamil) to refute all anthropomorphic views (madhahib al-mushabbiha). The author states he will first give a general proof, then specific refutations for each sect mentioned earlier. The general argument is: shape, form, measure, and change are all indications of contingency (huduth). If God had any of these, He would be contingent (muhdath). The rational basis: the mind sees all measures as equally possible, so any specific measure requires a particularizer (mukhassis). Similarly, change—when an essence becomes qualified by an attribute it did not have—proves it was not eternal, because change is a sign of contingency. The author uses the example: our own essence, when it acquires a new attribute, has changed, and change indicates it was originated. Therefore, beings are known to be contingent only through their change. In conclusion, change requires a changer (mghayyir) external to the changed thing, and determination requires a determiner (muqaddir). This refutes any view that assigns to God measure, shape, direction, or originated attributes, because all these entail contingency.

Key Terms

الأشكال
الصور
التغير

الجهة
المغير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'change' here means God changes His essence; but the argument is about the logical implication: if God had originated attributes in His essence, that would entail change and thus contingency.
Correction: Change is a sign of contingency; God does not change, nor does He have attributes that arise in time.

Checkpoint
What is the main premise of the author's general proof against anthropomorphism?
A) God cannot be divided into parts
B) Determination and change are proofs of contingency
C) The Quran prohibits anthropomorphism
D) All sects agree on divine transcendence

Key Takeaway
God's transcendence is rationally established because any determination, shape, or change would entail contingency, which is impossible for the Necessary Being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine God, do you unconsciously assign Him a form or direction in your mind? How does this rational argument expose that as a subtle form of anthropomorphism? What will you do to purge such thoughts?
?
Consider your own changes: you were once not wise, then became wise; not patient, then patient. God's essence cannot undergo such change. What does this unchanging nature demand from your worship and trust in Him?
?
If every measure requires a determiner, and God is not determined, how does that affect your understanding of His absolute freedom? In what areas of your life are you trying to 'determine' God—by expecting specific outcomes or forms of mercy—and how can you surrender to His transcendent will?

General Proof Against Anthropomorphism: Determination Implies Temporality
Author presents a universal proof that determination implies temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the universal proof that determination by shape and change necessarily implies temporality, and why this refutes anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
Determination requires a determiner; change requires a changer — both imply temporality.

This proof is the foundation for rejecting any anthropomorphic description of Allah. The author presents a logical argument that anything determined by shape, size, or subject to change must be temporal. Since Allah is eternal, He cannot possess such attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall argument. Notice the structure: the author states the proof, gives the reasoning, and concludes.
Pay attention to the key terms: تقدير (determination), تغير (change), حدوث (temporality).
2
Understand the core reasoning
Identify the premise: All sizes and shapes are equally possible in reason. Therefore, a specific size or shape needs a particularizer (مخصص). This shows that being determined implies being created or temporal.
The author uses the principle that specification requires a cause.
3
Examine the analogy with change
The author draws an analogy to ourselves: when we acquire a new quality, we change, and change is evidence of temporality. Similarly, if Allah were subject to change, He would be temporal.
This reinforces the main point that change implies temporality.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that the proof conclusively shows that Allah cannot be determined by shape, size, direction, or subject to change, because these would make Him temporal. This is a universal refutation of anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لنا دليل شامل يعم إبطال مذاهب المشبهة جملة، وعلى كل فرقة ممن عددناهم حجة خاصة ونقض على الانفراد فنبتدي بالأعم، ونقول: التقدير بالأشكال والصور والتغير بالحوادث والتغير دليل الحدوث، فلو كان الباري سبحانه متقدرًا بقدر، متصوراً بصورة متناهيا بحد ونهاية مختصاً بجهة، متغيرا بصفة حادثة في ذاته لكان محدثًا؛ إذ العقل بصريحه يقضي أن الأقدار في تجويز العقل متساوية، فما من قدر وشكل يقدره العقل إلا ويجوز أن يكون مخصوصاً بقدر آخر، واختصاصه بقدر معين وتميزه بجهة ومسافة يستدعي مخصصاً، ومن المعلوم الذي لا مراء فيه أن ذاتنا لم تكن موصوفة بصفة ثم صارت موصوفة فقد تغيرت عما كانت عليه، والتغير دليل الحدوث فإذا لم يستدل على حدوث الكاينات إلا بالتغير الطارئ عليها، وبالجملة فالتغير يستدعي مغيرًا خارجًا من ذات المغير، والمقدر يستدعي مقدرا.
We have a comprehensive proof that generally refutes the doctrines of the anthropomorphists, and for each sect among those we have enumerated there is a specific argument and a refutation individually. So we begin with the general and say: Determination by shapes and forms, and change by accidents, and change is evidence of temporality. So if the Creator, exalted, were determined by a size, imagined with a form, limited by a boundary and an end, characterized by a direction, changing by an accident occurring in His essence, then He would be temporal. For reason with its clarity judges that sizes are equal in the estimation of reason: no size or shape that reason can conceive but it is possible that it be particularized by another size, and its being particularized by a specific size and distinguished by a direction and distance requires a particularizer. And it is known without doubt that our essence was not described by a quality then became described by it, so it changed from what it was, and change is evidence of temporality. Since we only infer the temporality of generated things by the change that occurs upon them, and in summary, change requires a changer external to the essence of the changed, and the determined requires a determiner.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is a general proof (dhalil shamil) against the anthropomorphists (mushabbiha). The author states that his proof refutes all their doctrines collectively, then he will also give specific arguments against each sect. The proof begins with a premise: determination by shape (taqdir bi al-ashkal) and change (taghayyur) are evidence of temporality (huduth). If God were determined by size, shape, limit, direction, or subject to change by an accident, He would be temporal (muhdath). The reasoning is that reason judges all possible sizes and shapes to be equal in possibility; no particular size or shape is inherently more likely. Therefore, the fact that something has a specific size or shape requires a particularizer (mukhasis) that chose it over others. Next, the author draws an analogy to ourselves: when we acquire a new quality, we change, and change is evidence of temporality. Thus, if God changed, He would be temporal. The proof concludes that change requires a changer outside the essence that changes, and what is determined requires a determiner. The implication is that God cannot be subject to such determinations or changes.

حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any description of God implies anthropomorphism, so even attributing knowledge or power to God is problematic.
Correction: This proof specifically targets determination by shape, size, direction, and change by accidents. Essential attributes like knowledge and power are not subject to determination or change in the same way; they are eternal and necessary, not contingent on a particularizer.

Checkpoint

A) It is necessary by itself.
B) It requires a particularizer (mukhasis).
C) It is impossible for God.
D) It is an eternal attribute.

Key Takeaway
Determination by shape or change in attributes necessarily implies temporality, proving that Allah, being eternal, is free from such limitations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The proof shows that even the greatest intellect must admit that determination requires a determiner. Have you ever intellectually denied Allah's oneness because you couldn't conceive of a being without form? How will you submit your reason to this rational proof?

Objection on Two Self-Subsisting Existents and Direction
The author raises an objection about two self-subsisting existents and the implication of direction or containment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that two self-subsisting existents necessarily imply direction, and why this challenges the doctrine of divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Even distinct self-subsisting entities imply spatial relations—a challenge to God's transcendence.

This lesson presents an objection raised against the claim that God transcends direction. The objection argues that if two self-subsisting existents exist, they must be either adjacent or separate, each having a direction relative to the other. This seems to force God into spatial categories. Let's examine this reasoning closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Carefully read the Arabic and translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if two self-subsisting existents... then either adjacent or separate.'
The passage is incomplete; it cuts off while discussing being 'inside in essence'.
2
Identify the key terms
Identify terms like 'self-subsisting existents' (موجودان قائمان بأنفسهما), 'direction' (جهة), 'inside the world' (داخل العالم), 'outside the world' (خارج عن العالم). Understand their theological significance.
3
Analyze the logical structure
The objection uses a dichotomy: two distinct existents are either adjacent or separate. In both cases, each has a direction relative to the other. This implies that any two distinct entities must have spatial relations.
Think about how this applies to the Creator and creation.
4
Connect to the theological issue
The objection is aimed at showing that if God is distinct from the world, then He must be either inside it or outside it, implying direction. This challenges the Ash'ari position that God is beyond all direction.
A later response would need to explain why this reasoning does not apply to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: إذا قدر موجودان قائمان بأنفسهما بحيث لا يكون أحدهما بحيث يكون الثاني، كالعرض في الجوهر، فمن الضرورة إما أن يكونا متجاورين أو إما أن يكون متباينين، وعلى الوجهين جميعا يجب أن يكون كل واحد منهما بجهة من الثاني، وربما عبروا عن هذا المعنى بأن قالوا: الباري سبحانه لا يخلو إما أن يكون داخل العالم أو خارجا عن العالم، وكما أن الدخول بالذات يقتضي
For if it is said: If two self-subsisting existents are supposed, such that one is not such that it is the other—like an accident in a substance—then of necessity either they are adjacent or they are separate. In both cases, it is necessary that each one of them be in a direction (jihah) relative to the other. And perhaps they expressed this meaning by saying: The Exalted Creator is not free from either being inside the world or outside the world. And just as being inside in essence requires...
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage presents an objection (qīla) against a previous position, likely relating to God's transcendence beyond direction. The objection postulates two self-subsisting existents (mawjūdān qāʾimān bi-anfusihimā) that are distinct and not identical. The example given is like an accident in a substance, though the text may mean the relationship between two substances. By necessity, such two existents must be either adjacent (mutajāwirān) or separate (mutabāyinān). In either case, each would have a direction (jihah) relative to the other. This leads to the common theological formulation: God is either inside the world or outside the world. The text begins to discuss the implications of being 'inside in essence' (al-dukhūl bi al-dhāt), but the passage cuts off. The objection aims to challenge the claim that God transcends spatial relations entirely, as the very existence of two distinct entities seems to entail relational direction.

Key Terms

الجهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is a conclusive proof that God must have a direction.
Correction: This is an objection that the author will refute. The Ash'ari position maintains that God is beyond direction, and the objection does not apply because God is not a body and does not enter into spatial relationships.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The objection argues that any two distinct self-subsisting entities must have spatial relations, which poses a challenge to the idea of a transcendent God beyond direction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The objection assumes two self-subsisting existents must have direction. But God is utterly unique. Ask yourself: Am I ever guilty of comparing God to created things in my mind, treating Him as just another 'being' rather than the Absolute Being? How can you remind yourself daily of His incomparability?

Objection on the Necessity of Direction for Self-Subsisting Existence
Objection that a self-subsisting being must have a direction; answer begins with equivocation on 'self-subsisting'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that a self-subsisting being must have a direction, and how the answer begins by clarifying the meaning of 'self-subsisting' for God.

Core Idea
The objection wrongly assumes 'self-subsisting' implies spatial direction.

This passage presents a powerful objection against God's transcendence: if God is self-subsisting, then He must have a direction relative to other beings. The objector uses logical and scriptural arguments. The answer begins by exposing an equivocation in the term 'self-subsisting'—for God it means independence, not spatial location. Let's trace the objection carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the logical flow from assumption to conclusion.
The objector uses analogies like adjacent/separate and inside/outside.
2
Identify the key analogies
The objector first argues that two self-subsistents are either adjacent or separate, both entailing direction. Then applies this to God being inside or outside the world.
These analogies assume that self-subsistence implies spatial relations.
3
Examine the argument from attributes
The objector argues that attributes have no direction, so the essence must have a 'where' to be distinct from attributes. Then if another self-subsistent existed, it would need a direction relative to God.
This is meant to show that God must have a direction by rational necessity.
4
See how the answer begins
The answer starts by attributing the confusion to equivocation on 'self-subsisting': for God, it means independence (istighna), not a spatial mode of existence.
The full answer is cut off, but the key move is clarifying the term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : إذا قدر موجودان قايمان بأنفسهما بحيث لا يكون أحدهما بحيث يكون الثاني ؛ كالعرض في الجوهر ) ، فمن الضرورة إما أن يكونا متجاورين وإما أن يكون متباينين، وعلى الوجهين جميعًا يجب أن يكون كل واحد منهما بجهة من الثاني، وربما عبروا عن هذا المعنى بأن قالوا : الباري سبحانه لا يخلو إما أن يكون داخل العالم أو خارجًا عن العالم، وكما أن الدخول بالذات يقتضي مجاورة ومماسة، والخروج بالذات يستدعي مباينة وجهةً، وربما شككوا أبلغ تشكيك على سبيل الإلزام. فقالوا : اتفقنا على أن له سبحانه ذاتا وصفات، ومن المعلوم أن الصفات لا تكون كل واحدة بحيث الثانية ولا منحازة عنها بجهة، فإن القايم بالغير لا يقبل التحيز رأسا ، بل هي كلها قايمة بذاته ؛ أي بحيث ذاته، فقد تحقق التميز بين الذات والصفات، وذلك راجع إلى أن الذات لها حيث حتى تكون الصفات بحيث هو، والصفات لا حيث لها، فلا تكون الذات بحيث هي، وما قيل التحيث بالنسبة إلى الصفات، فلو قدر قايم بذات آخر فمن ضرورته أن لا يكون بحيث هو حتى يتحقق فرق بين الصفات التي هي بحيث هو، وبين ذلك القائم بذاته الذي ليس بحيث هو فيثبت له جهة ما ينحاز بها عنه، وقد ورد السمع بأن تلك الجهة هي جهة فوق، قال الله تعالى : ( وَهُوَ الْقَاهِرُ فَوْقَ عِبَادِهِ ) فأثبتنا الجهة عقلا وأثبتنا الفوقية سمعا ، واستنبطنا من النص الوارد فيه معنى وهو كون الفوق أشرف الجهات وأليق بكمال الصمدية، ولهذا تعلقت القلوب بالسماء ورفعت الأيدي ودلت عليها إشارة الخرساء وإليها كان معراج سيد الأنبياء. والجواب: قلنا: هذه الشبهات كلها نشأت من اشتراك في لفظ القايم بالنفس، فإن عندنا يطلق هذا اللفظ في حق الباري سبحانه بمعنى أنه مستغن عن ...
For if it is said: If there are two existents subsisting by themselves such that neither is such that the second is [like the accident in the substance], then necessarily either they are adjacent or they are separate. In both cases, each must be in a direction relative to the other. They sometimes express this meaning by saying: The Exalted Creator is either inside the world or outside the world. Just as being inside by essence entails adjacency and contact, being outside by essence demands separation and direction. And they raised the strongest doubts by way of entailment. They said: We agree that He has essence and attributes. It is known that the attributes are not each such that the second is [like that] nor are they separated by a direction, for that which subsists in another does not accept spatial location at all. Rather they all subsist by His essence, i.e., in the manner of His essence. Thus distinction between essence and attributes is realized, and that reduces to the essence having a 'where' such that the attributes are in the manner of it, while the attributes have no 'where', so the essence is not in the manner of them. What is said about 'where-ness' is relative to attributes. So if another self-subsisting were supposed, it would necessarily not be in the manner of Him so that a difference could be realized between the attributes which are in His manner and that self-subsisting which is not in His manner. Thus a direction is established for it by which it is separate from Him. And scripture has come that that direction is the direction of 'above'. God said: 'And He is the All-Overcomer above His servants.' So we affirmed direction by reason and affirmed 'above-ness' by scripture, and we derived from the text a meaning: that 'above' is the noblest of directions and most fitting for the perfection of Self-Sufficiency. For this reason hearts are attached to the sky and hands are raised and the mute points to it, and to it was the Night Journey of the Master of the Prophets. The answer: We say: These doubts all arise from equivocation in the term 'self-subsisting' ('al-qa'im bi-nafs'), for according to us this term is applied to the Exalted Creator in the sense that He is independent of ...
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is an objection and the beginning of an answer regarding the direction of God. The objector argues that a self-subsisting being must have a direction relative to another self-subsisting being. He uses analogies: two self-subsistents are either adjacent or separate, and both entail direction. He then applies this to God: either inside or outside the world. Attributes are inseparable from essence and have no direction, so essence has a 'where' distinct from attributes. Then if another self-subsistent existed, it must have a direction to be distinguished from attributes. The objector cites scripture (Quran 6:18) to support that God is 'above'. He concludes that reason proves a direction and scripture specifies it as 'above'. The answer begins by attributing the confusion to equivocation on 'self-subsisting': for God, it means independence, not a spatial location. The answer is cut off, so the full refutation is not given.

Key Terms
قائم بالنفس
جهة
فوقية
مجاورة
مباينة
صمدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A self-subsisting being must have a spatial direction relative to other beings.
Correction: For God, 'self-subsisting' means absolute independence (al-istighna), not a spatial mode of existence. The objection equivocates between physical and metaphysical self-subsistence.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, what does the existence of another self-subsistent entail?
A) No difference.
B) Direction relative to it.
C) Identity with it.
D) Non-existence.

Key Takeaway
The objection's strength depends on a spatial reading of 'self-subsisting', which the answer rejects by clarifying that God's self-subsistence is absolute independence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine God, what picture forms in your mind? Are you inadvertently assigning a direction or location to Him? How can you train yourself to think of Him as beyond all spatial categories, as this lesson implies?
?
The objector's argument seemed logical, but it relied on an ambiguous term. In your own life, have you ever insisted on a certain understanding of God that was actually based on a mistaken assumption? Will you repent and seek the correct understanding, even if it humbles you?
?
The passage says that hearts are attached to the sky and hands are raised. But is your heart truly attached to the transcendent God Himself, or merely to a comfortable mental image of Him 'up there'? What would it cost you to purify your worship from such subtle anthropomorphism?

The Objection and the Ambiguity of 'Qaim bi-Nafsih'
Presents the objection and the answer identifying equivocation in 'qaim bi-nafsih'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that God must have a direction and the key answer that the confusion arises from the term 'self-subsistent' being used differently for God and for substances.

Core Idea
The term 'self-subsistent' has two meanings; confusing them leads to false conclusions about God's direction.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection against divine transcendence and the beginning of the answer. The opponents argue that God's attributes require a locus, necessitating a direction, and they cite scripture. The author, however, reveals that the entire objection hinges on a verbal ambiguity in the term 'qaim bi-nafsih'. By clarifying the two distinct meanings, the author dismantles the objection at its root.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection carefully. Note how the opponents reason from the distinction between essence and attributes to a necessary direction, and how they use scripture to support their claim.
The objection is built on the assumption that attributes require a locus (hayth).
2
Identify the term in dispute
Find where the author says the objection arises from ambiguity in 'qaim bi-nafsih'. Underline the two meanings he gives.
3
Contrast the two meanings
Compare the meaning for God (independent of locus and space) versus for a substance (independent of locus only). Ask yourself: what difference does 'space' (hayz) make?
This distinction is the central move in the answer.
4
Reflect on the objection after clarification
After reading the answer, reconsider the objection. Does it still hold if we use the correct meaning for God? Why or why not?
The author claims the opponents have used the term incorrectly.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مجاورة وومماسة)، والخروج بالذات يستدعي مباينة وجهةً، وربما شككوا أبلغ تشكيك على سبيل الإلزام.
فقالوا: اتفقنا على أن له سبحانه ذاتا وصفات، ومن المعلوم أن الصفات لا تكون كل واحدة بحيث الثانية ولا منحازة عنها بجهة، فإن القائم بالغير لا يقبل التحيز رأسا، بل هي كلها قائمة بذاته؛ أي بحيث ذاته، فقد تحقق التميز بين الذات والصفات، وذلك راجع إلى أن الذات لها حيث حتى تكون الصفات بحيث هو، والصفات لا حيث لها، فلا تكون الذات بحيث هي، وما قيل التحيز بالنسبة إلى الصفات، فلو قدر قائم بذات آخر فمن ضرورته أن لا يكون بحيث هو حتى يتحقق فرق بين الصفات التي هي بحيث هو، وبين ذلك القائم بذاته الذي ليس بحيث هو فيثبت له جهة ما ينحاز بها عنه، وقد ورد السمع بأن تلك الجهة هي جهة فوق، قال الله تعالى: (وَهُوَ الْقَاهِرُ فَوْقَ عِبَادِهِ) [الأنعام: 18] فأثبتنا الجهة عقلا وأثبتنا الفوقية سمعا، واستنبطنا من النص الوارد فيه معنى وهو كون الفوق أشرف الجهات وأليق بكمال الصمدية، ولهذا تعلقت القلوب بالسماء ورفعت الأيدي ودلت عليها إشارة الخرساء وإليها كان معراج سيد الأنبياء.

والجواب: قلنا: هذه الشبهات كلها نشأت من اشتراك في لفظ القائم بالنفس، فإن عندنا يطلق هذا اللفظ في حق الباري سبحانه بمعنى أنه مستغن عن المحل والحيز جميعًا، ويطلق على الجوهر بمعنى أنه مستغن عن المحل فقط، والمستغنى على الإطلاق في مقابلة المحتاج على الإطلاق، والمستغنى عن المحل والحيز في مقابلة المحتاج إلى المحل والمحتاج إلى الحيز، فلننقل العبارة إلى هذه الجهة حتى يتبين أنكم جعلتم نفس النزاع دليلا متمسكين باشتراك في العبارة دون المعنى، ونقول: قدرناه مستغنيا عن المحل والحيز ومحتاجا إلى الحيز، فيجب أن يكونا؛ إما متجاورين أو متباينين محال تقديره، فإن التجاور والتباين من لوازم التحيز في المتحيزات، فالمستغني عن التحيز كيف يكون إما متجاورا وإما متباينا، هذا كمن يقول: القائمان بأنفسهما؛ إما أن يكونا مجتمعين أو مفترقين، متحركين أو ساكنين، قيل: الاجتماع والافتراق من لوازم التحيز والتحدد ولا حيز له سبحانه ولا حد ولا اجتماع ولا افتراق بل إذا فتش على المجاورة والمباينة لم يتحقق منه إلا نفس الاجتماع والافتراق وما جاور أو بأين فقد تناهى ذاتا، والمتناهي إذا اختص بمقدار استدعي مخصصا.

وكذلك الجواب عن الدخول والخروج فإنَّا نقول: ليس بداخل في العالم ولا خارج؛ لأن الدخول والخروج من لوازم المتحيزات والمحدودات، ولهذا لا يطلقان على الأعراض وهما كالاجتماع والافتراق والحركة والسكون، وسائر الأعراض التي لا تختص بالأجرام التي لا حياة لها، ولو قيل: هو الله سبحانه داخل في العالم بمعنى العلم والقدرة، وخارج عن العالم بمعنى التقدس والتنزيه كان معنى صحيحًا، كما ورد في التنزيل: (وَهُوَ الْقَاهِرُ فَوْقَ عِبَادِهِ) [الأنعام: 18]، وقد ورد (وَهُوَ مَعَكُمْ أَيْنَ مَا كُنْتُمْ) [الحديد: 4]، (وَنَحْنُ أَقْرَبُ إِلَيْهِ مِنْ حَبْلِ الْوَرِيدِ) [ق: 16].
(Contiguity and contact), and exiting by essence necessitates distinctness in direction, and perhaps they raised the strongest doubts by way of binding objection.
They said: We have agreed that He, exalted, has an essence and attributes, and it is known that the attributes are not each one in the locus (hayth) of the second nor separated from it by a direction, for the one subsisting through another does not accept occupying space (tahayyuz) at all; rather they all subsist through His essence, i.e., in the locus of His essence. So distinction between the essence and attributes is realized, and that reverts to the essence having a locus such that the attributes are in His locus, and the attributes have no locus, so the essence is not in their locus. And what is said of locus (tahayyuz) with respect to the attributes, if one supposes a subsistent through another essence, then it is necessary that it not be in His locus in order to realize a difference between the attributes which are in His locus and that subsistent through its own essence which is not in His locus; so a direction is established for it by which it is separated from Him. And the transmitted (sam') has come that that direction is the direction of above, Allah said: (And He is the Victor over His servants) [Al-An'am 6:18]. So we affirmed direction by reason and affirmed the aboveness (fawqiyya) by transmission, and we derived from the text a meaning, that the above is the most honorable of directions and most suitable for the perfection of self-sufficiency (samadiyya); therefore hearts are attached to the sky and hands are raised and it is indicated by the gesture of the mute, and to it was the ascension of the master of the prophets.

The answer: We said: All these doubts arose from ambiguity in the term 'self-subsistent' (qaim bi-nafsih), for in our view this term is used for the Creator, exalted, meaning that He is independent of both locus (mahall) and space (hayz), and it is used for a substance (jawhar) meaning that it is independent of locus only. The absolutely independent is in contrast to the absolutely dependent, and the independent of locus and space is in contrast to the dependent on locus and dependent on space. So let us shift the expression to this aspect so that it becomes clear that you have made the very dispute a proof, clinging to an ambiguity in expression without meaning. And we say: Suppose it independent of locus and space but dependent on space; then it must be either contiguous or separate – that is impossible to suppose. For contiguity and separation are necessary concomitants of occupying space in what occupies space. So how can that which is independent of occupying space be either contiguous or separate? This is like one who says: The two self-subsistents must be either together or apart, moving or still. It is said: Togetherness and apartness are necessary concomitants of occupying space and being delimited, and He, exalted, has no space, no limit, no togetherness, no apartness. Rather, if one examines contiguity and separation, nothing is realized from them except togetherness and apartness itself. Whatever is contiguous or separate (ba'ina) has become finite in essence, and what is finite, if it is distinguished by a magnitude, demands a particularizer (mukhassis).

And likewise the answer regarding entering and exiting: we say He is not inside the world nor outside, because entering and exiting are necessary concomitants of what occupies space and is delimited; therefore they are not applied to accidents (a'rad) and they are like togetherness and apartness, movement and stillness, and the rest of the accidents which are not specific to bodies that have no life. And if it is said: He, Allah, is inside the world in the sense of knowledge and power, and outside the world in the sense of sanctity and transcendence, that is a correct meaning, as came in the revelation: (And He is the Victor over His servants) [Al-An'am 6:18], and it came (And He is with you wherever you are) [Al-Hadid 57:4], (And We are closer to him than the jugular vein) [Qaf 50:16].
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The author begins by presenting an objection posed by opponents who argue that God must have a direction (jiha) because His attributes are distinct from His essence and require a 'locus' (hayth), and since scriptural evidence mentions 'above', they claim to have proven a direction for God by reason and scripture.

The answer then systematically dismantles the objection. First, the author identifies the root of the confusion: the term 'self-subsistent' (qaim bi-nafsih) is used equivocally for God and for substances. For God, it means independence from both locus (mahall) and space (hayz). For a substance (jawhar), it means independence only from a locus, not from space. The opponents have conflated these meanings, forcing God into the category of things that occupy space.

Second, the author shows the logical impossibility: if God were independent of space but dependent on space (a contradiction from the opponents' assumption), He would have to be either contiguous or separate—properties that only apply to beings that occupy space. Since God is independent of space, these categories do not apply.

Third, the author addresses the specific issue of 'entering' and 'exiting' the world, stating that these are properties of what occupies space and are not applied to God. But a metaphorical interpretation is allowed: 'inside' in terms of knowledge and power, 'outside' in terms of transcendence. The scriptural verses about 'above' and 'with you' are interpreted in a way that respects divine transcendence.

Throughout, the author emphasizes that the opponents have mistaken verbal ambiguity for a genuine rational proof.

Key Terms
qaim bi-nafsih
tahayyuz
jiha
fawqiyya
samadiyya
hayz
mahall

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'self-subsistent' (qaim bi-nafsih) means the same thing for God and for created substances.
Correction: In kalam, it is used equivocally: for God it means independence from both locus and space; for substances it means independence only from a locus.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the root cause of the objection that God must have a direction?
A) The literal interpretation of verses about 'above'
B) The confusion between the term 'self-subsistent' applied to God vs. substances
C) The assumption that attributes are separate from the essence
D) The reliance on reason rather than scripture

Key Takeaway
The objection that God requires a direction is based on a verbal ambiguity; clarifying the term 'qaim bi-nafsih' shows the argument is unsound.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God is above' in duʿā or thought, do you unconsciously imagine a spatial direction? How does the clarification in this lesson challenge that mental image, and what can you do to purify your concept of God?
?
The opponents' mistake was confusing terms. Are there terms or descriptions of God that you have been using without fully understanding their intended meaning? Will you commit to learning the precise theological meanings to avoid error?
?
If God is truly independent of space, how should that change the way you sense His closeness when you pray? Does your heart need to shift from thinking of a 'place' to a 'presence' without location?

The Logical Impossibility of Direction and the Meaning of 'Inside' and 'Outside'
Continues the answer with the contiguity/separation argument and the inside/outside clarification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that God cannot be described as contiguous or separate, and the proper interpretation of scriptural language about 'aboveness' and 'withness'.

Core Idea
God, being independent of space, cannot be subject to properties like contiguity, separation, entering, or exiting.

Having exposed the verbal ambiguity, the author now demonstrates the logical impossibility of God having a direction. He shows that if God were to have a direction, He would have to be either contiguous or separate—categories that only apply to spatial beings. He then addresses the terms 'inside' and 'outside', offering a metaphorical interpretation that preserves transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the contiguity argument
Read the passage starting from 'And we say: Suppose it independent of locus and space but dependent on space...' Follow the reasoning that leads to an impossible conclusion.
The author forces the opponent's assumption to its logical absurdity.
2
Identify the key properties
Note which properties are said to be concomitants of occupying space (tahayyuz): contiguity (mujawara), separation (mubayana), togetherness (ijtima'), apartness (iftiraq), movement, stillness, entering, exiting.
3
Understand the inside/outside section
Read the final part about 'inside' and 'outside'. The author states that these are inapplicable to God literally, but can be interpreted metaphorically in terms of knowledge/power (inside) and transcendence (outside).
Metaphorical interpretation resolves apparent scriptural contradictions.
4
Connect to the overall answer
See how this section completes the answer. After clarifying the term, the author now shows that even if we follow the opponent's logic, it leads to absurdity, and then he provides a sound interpretation of the relevant verses.

?

?

How will you change your prayer so that you are truly addressing the Transcendent One, not a mental image?
?

Are you ready to uproot that assumption and replace it with a closeness that is solely by knowledge and power? What will you do today to start thinking of His nearness in a way worthy of His transcendence?

Divine Transcendence
intermediate
6 min
Refuting Spatial Adjacency and Separation for God
Sanusi refutes attributing spatial adjacency or separation to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why applying spatial adjacency or separation to God is logically impossible, as it presupposes spatial occupation.

Core Idea
Adjacency and separation only apply to spatial beings; God is beyond space.

In this passage, the author tackles a subtle theological error: the assumption that God must be either spatially adjacent to the world or separate from it. By analyzing the meaning of these terms, he shows that they are properties of beings that occupy space. Since God is free from spatial location, such descriptions are meaningless. This lesson will walk through his logical refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the author's rhetorical move: he reorients the discussion to expose confusion in terms.
2
Identify key terms
Look for the terms تجاور (adjacency), تباين (separation), تحيز (spatial occupation). Understand their relationship: adjacency and separation are necessary concomitants of spatial occupation.
These terms are central to the argument.
3
Understand the logical structure
The author supposes a being independent of place but in need of direction. Logically, such a being would have to be adjacent or separate, but this is impossible because both are properties of spatial things. The conclusion: the supposition is contradictory.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Analyze the analogy
The author compares to two self-subsisting entities being together or apart. The response shows that togetherness and separation are also properties of spatial things, not applicable to non-spatial beings.
This reinforces the point using a familiar example.
5
Reflect on the final implication
The passage ends by noting that if something is adjacent or separate, it is finite and limited, which would require a cause. For God, this would imply dependence, which is impossible.
This ties the argument to divine transcendence and the need for a particularizer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلننقل العبارة إلى هذه الجهة حتى يتبين أنكم جعلتم نفس النزاع دليلا متمسكين باشتراك في العبارة دون المعنى، ونقول: قدرناه مستغنيا عن المحل والحيز ومحتاجًا إلى الحيز، فيجب أن يكونا إما متجاورين أو متباينين محال تقديره، فإن التجاور والتباين من لوازم التحيز في المتحيزات، فالمستغني عن التحيز كيف يكون إما متجاوراً وإما متباينا، هذا كمن يقول: القايمان بأنفسهما إما أن يكونا مجتمعين أو مفترقين، متحركين أو ساكنين، قيل: الاجتماع والافتراق من لوازم التحيز والتحدد ولا حيز له سبحانه ولا حد ولا اجتماع ولا افتراق بل إذا فتش على المجاورة والمباينة لم يتحقق منه إلا نفس الاجتماع والافتراق وما جاور أو بأين فقد تناهى ذاتا، والمتناهي إذا اختص بمقدار استدعي مخصصاً.
Let us shift the expression to this aspect so that it becomes clear that you made the very dispute a proof, clinging to a sharing in expression without meaning. We say: we supposed it to be independent of place and direction but in need of direction, so it must be either adjacent or separate, which is impossible to suppose. For adjacency and separation are among the necessary concomitants of spatial occupation in spatial things. How can that which is independent of spatial occupation be either adjacent or separate? This is like someone who says: the two things existing by themselves are either together or apart, moving or still, and it would be said: togetherness and separation are among the necessary concomitants of spatial occupation and delimitation, and He has no spatial location, no limit, no togetherness, no separation. Rather, if one investigates adjacency and separation, nothing is realized from it except togetherness and separation themselves. Whatever is adjacent or separated is finite in essence, and the finite, when it is restricted to a measure, requires a particularizer.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who argue that God must be either adjacent to the world or separate from it in a spatial sense. The author begins by noting that the opponents have turned the very point of dispute into a proof by equivocating on terms. The author then supposes a being that is independent of place and direction but in need of direction (i.e., spatial orientation). Such a being would have to be either adjacent or separate from another being, but this is impossible because adjacency and separation are properties of things that occupy space (tahayyuz). A being that is free from spatial occupation cannot be described in these terms. The author provides an analogy: if someone says that two self-subsisting entities must be either together or apart, moving or still, the response is that togetherness and separation are properties of spatial things. God has no spatial location, so these categories do not apply. The passage concludes that if something is adjacent or separated, it is finite in essence, and a finite thing restricted to a measure requires a cause (mukhassis) to particularize it, which would imply dependence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'adjacent' and 'separate' are neutral descriptions that can be applied to God without implying spatiality.
Correction: Adjacency and separation are logically tied to spatial occupation. Since God is not spatial, these terms are inapplicable and their use leads to absurdities.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider it impossible for God to be either adjacent or separate from the world?
A) Because God is infinite and cannot be adjacent to anything
B) Because adjacency and separation are properties of spatial beings, and God is free from spatial location
C) Because God is everywhere and therefore cannot be separate
D) Because the Quran explicitly denies both

Key Takeaway
God cannot be described as spatially adjacent or separate because these terms necessarily imply spatial occupation, which God transcends.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that God cannot be described as adjacent or separate because He transcends space. Think of a time when you felt God was 'distant' or 'close' in a spatial way. How does this lesson challenge that feeling, and what does it mean for your personal experience of closeness to God? Will you change how you think about your relationship with Him?
?
The passage says that whatever is finite requires a particularizer—a cause that makes it what it is. You are finite, limited in time and space. Does your daily life reflect a deep awareness that you are completely dependent on God as your particularizer, or do you act as if you are self-sufficient? What can you do today to live in that dependence?
?
Sanusi points out that people use the same words but with different meanings, leading to error. In what areas of your faith might you be assuming that God's attributes are like human attributes without realizing the radical difference?
