God is Neither Inside nor Outside the World
The author explains that inside/outside are spatial concepts and God is neither, citing Quranic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why spatial concepts like inside/outside do not apply to God and how Quranic verses about nearness and above-ness are to be interpreted.

Core Idea
God is beyond space and time; spatial language in the Quran is figurative.

This passage from al-Shahrastani addresses a common objection: if God is not inside the world, is He then outside it? The author explains that both categories belong only to material things. He then shows how Quranic expressions like 'above' and 'with' are to be understood without attributing spatiality to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal English translation. Let the author's argument flow naturally.
Notice the density of Quranic citations towards the end.
2
Identify the key refutation
The author first states that 'inside' and 'outside' are properties of spatial objects, then allows a figurative meaning. Identify where he makes this distinction.
Look for the phrase 'ليس بداخل في العالم ولا خارج'.
3
Reflect on the Quranic evidence
Consider the verses cited about 'above', 'with', and 'near'. Ask yourself how they relate to the author's conclusion that God is not spatial.
The author notes that nearness verses are more numerous than 'above' verses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك الجواب عن الدخول والخروج فإنَّا نقول: ليس بداخل في العالم ولا خارج؛ لأن الدخول والخروج من لوازم المتحيزات والمحدودات، ولهذا لا يطلقان على الأعراض وهما كالاجتماع والافتراق والحركة والسكون، وسائر الأعراض التي لا تختص بالأجرام التي لا حياة لها، ولو قيل: هو الله سبحانه داخل في العالم بمعنى العلم والقدرة، وخارج عن العالم بمعنى التقدس والتنزيه كان معنى صحيحًا، كما ورد في التنزيل: {وَهُوَ الْقَاهِرُ فَوْقَ عِبَادِهِ} [الأنعام: 18]، وقد ورد {وَهُوَ مَعَكُمْ أَيْنَ مَا كُنْتُمْ} [الحديد: 4]، {وَنَحْنُ أَقْرَبُ إِلَيْهِ مِنْ حَبْلِ الْوَرِيدِ} [ق: 16] وقوله سبحانه: {إِنِّي قَرِيبٌ أُجِيبُ دَعْوَةَ الدَّاعِ إِذَا دَعَانِ} [البقرة: 186]. بل الآيات الدالة على القرب أكثر من الآيات الدالة على بعد الفوق، وكما ورد: {وَهُوَ الَّذِي فِي السَّمَاءِ إِلَهٌ} [الزخرف: 84] وورد مقرونا به: {وَفِي الْأَرْضِ إِلَهٌ}، وبالجملة من تصور وجوده وجودًا مكانيًا طلب له جهة ما، وانحيازا عن العالم ببينونة متناهية أو غير متناهية، كما أن من تخيل وجوده زمانيا طلب له مدة وتقدما على العالم بأزمنة متناهية أو غير متناهية وكلا التخيلين باطل، فهو الأول والآخر إذ ليس وجوده سبحانه زمانيا والظاهر والباطن إذ ليس وجوده مكانيًا.
And likewise the answer concerning entering and exiting: we say: He is not inside the world nor outside, because entering and exiting are among the necessary concomitants of spatial and limited things. For this reason they are not applied to accidents, and they are like togetherness, separation, motion, and rest, and the rest of the accidents that are not specific to bodies that have no life. And if it were said: He, exalted be He, is inside the world in the sense of knowledge and power, and outside the world in the sense of sanctity and transcendence, that would be a correct meaning. As came down in revelation: 'And He is the Subduer above His servants' (Quran 6:18), and it came: 'And He is with you wherever you are' (Quran 57:4), 'And We are nearer to him than the jugular vein' (Quran 50:16), and His saying: 'Indeed I am near, I answer the call of the caller when he calls Me' (Quran 2:186). Rather, the verses indicating nearness are more numerous than the verses indicating distance of above. And as it came: 'And He is the God in the heaven' (Quran 43:84), and accompanied by it: 'And in the earth a God.' In short, whoever conceives His existence as a spatial existence seeks for Him some direction and separation from the world by finite or infinite distinctness, just as whoever imagines His existence as temporal seeks for Him a duration and priority over the world by finite or infinite times. Both imaginations are false. So He is the First and the Last, since His existence is not temporal, and the Outward and the Inward, since His existence is not spatial.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is a response to the objection that God must be either inside or outside the world. The author states that being inside or outside are properties of things that have spatial extension and limits. For this reason, these terms are not applied even to accidents (like colour or sound) that are not spatial. If someone interprets 'inside' as God's knowledge encompassing the world and 'outside' as His transcendence, that is a valid figurative meaning. The author then cites multiple Quranic verses about God's 'above-ness' (fawq), 'with-ness', and 'nearness' (qurb) to show that these are not to be taken literally. Verses indicating nearness actually outnumber those indicating a physical 'above'. The author also mentions the verse that God is both the God in heaven and in earth, showing that He is not confined to one place. The passage concludes that conceiving of God's existence as spatial or temporal leads to false dichotomies of finite/infinite distance or time. God is beyond both space and time: as al-Awwal and al-Akhir He is not temporal, and as al-Zahir and al-Batin He is not spatial.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Quranic expressions like 'above' and 'with' imply spatial location for God.
Correction: These expressions are figurative; God is not limited by space or direction.

Checkpoint

B) By explaining that both are figurative expressions and God is not spatial
C) By stating that God is both literally above and with simultaneously
D) By ignoring the 'with' verses

Key Takeaway
God is not in space, and Quranic spatial language must be understood figuratively.

God's Transcendence of Direction
Al-Shahrastani explains that directions depend on finite bodies and refutes direction for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why direction is a property of finite bodies and how that refutes any claim that Allah is located in a literal direction.

Core Idea
Direction belongs only to finite bodies; Allah is infinite, so He has no direction.

This passage from al-Shahrastani's Nihayat al-Aqdam tackles a fundamental point of Islamic theology: does God occupy a direction? The author argues that direction is a property of finite, bounded bodies. After establishing that, he shows how the idea of God being 'above' leads to contradictions, especially given a spherical world. He then clarifies that devotional practices like raising hands to the sky do not imply spatial location for God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Pay attention to key terms like 'جهات', 'نهايات', and 'كرية'.
The author uses a concise style; reading twice helps.
2
Understand the key premise: directions are rulings of ends
The first sentence states that directions are among the rulings of ends (nihayaat) in bodies. That means direction applies only to bodies that have boundaries. If a body were infinite, direction would be meaningless.
This is the foundational principle for the entire argument.
3
Follow the spherical world argument
The passage then discusses spherical bodies, where above and below are relative to center and circumference. Applying this to the world, if the world is spherical, claiming God is in a direction forces Him to encompass the world or leaves a part of the world without Him, and 'above' becomes contradictory (what is above for one is below for another).
This shows the impossibility of a literal direction for God.
4
Reflect on the resolution of devotional practices
The passage addresses the objections from raising hands to the sky or the Prophet's ascension. It counters with acts like placing the head on the ground and explains that the Throne is the qibla of supplication, the earth is the mosque, and closeness to God is in prostration.
Worship practices have spiritual, not spatial, significance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة فليعلم أن جهات الأجسام من أحكام النهايات في الأجسام، حتى لو قدر مقدر جسما غير متناه بالفعل لم يكن للجهات معنى، فلا يكون فوق وتحت ويمين ويسار وقدام وخلف، ولذلك تتحقق إليها الإشارة، ولذواتها اختصاص وانفراد من جهة أخرى، وإذا كانت الأجسام كرية أي مدورة - فيكون تجدد الجهات على سبيل المحيط والمحاط والفوق والسفل فيها على سبيل المركز والمحيط، فإن قدر العالم كري الشكل؛ إما تقديرًا أوجد عليه حقيقة، وإما تقديرا يجوز أن يوجد عليه تصورا، قيل: إن الباري سبحانه بجهة من العالم فيكون لا محالة محيطا بكل العالم، وإلا فيخلو عن ذاته طرف من جهات الفوق، وعندهم هو فوق العالم بأسره فهو خلف، فيلزم عليه فوقا بالنسبة إلى من هو على الأرض على موازاة القطب الشمالي، وتحت بالنسبة إلى من هو عليها على موازاة القطب الجنوبي، بل يكون بعض منه فوقا وبعض منه تحتا وذلك محال، وإما تعلق القلوب بالسماء ورفع الأيدي إليها والمعراج إليها فمقابل بتعلق الرءوس بالأرض ووضع الأيدي عليها والنزول إليها، بل العرش قبلة الدعاء، والأرض مسجد الصلاة، والكعبة وجهة الوجه، وموضع السجود قبلة العين وأقرب ما يكون العبد من الرب إذا كان في السجود ( وَاسْجُدْ وَاقْتَرِب).
And in summary, let it be known that the directions (jihaat) of bodies are among the rulings of ends (nihayaat) in bodies, so much so that if someone were to suppose a body actually infinite, directions would have no meaning; thus there would be no above, below, right, left, front, or back. Therefore reference is realized to them [i.e., directions], and they [bodies] have particularity and exclusivity from another aspect. And if bodies are spherical (kulliyya? actually 'kulliyya'? the text says 'كرية أي مدورة' meaning spherical), then the renewal of directions occurs on the basis of the encompassing and the encompassed, and above and below in them are on the basis of center and circumference. For if the world is supposed to be spherical in shape—either as an existent form or as a conceptual possibility—then it is said: if the Creator, glorified be He, is in a direction of the world, then He must be encompassing the entire world; otherwise, a side of the directions of above would be devoid of His essence. And according to them (the anthropomorphists), He is above the entire world, so that would be inconsistent. For it would necessitate being above relative to someone on the earth along the axis of the North Pole, and below relative to someone on the earth along the axis of the South Pole; rather, some of it would be above and some of it below, and that is impossible. As for the attachment of hearts to the sky, the raising of hands to it, and the ascension to it—these are countered by the attachment of heads to the earth, the placing of hands upon it, and the descent to it. Rather, the Throne is the qibla of supplication, the earth is the mosque of prayer, the Ka'ba is the direction of the face, and the place of prostration is the qibla of the eye; and the closest that a servant comes to his Lord is when he is in prostration, as the verse says: 'Prostrate and draw near.'
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is a sustained argument demonstrating that direction (jiha) is a property of finite bodies, and therefore cannot be attributed to God. It begins by stating that directions are among the rulings of ends (nihayaat) in bodies, meaning direction applies only to bounded bodies. If a body were actually infinite, direction would be meaningless. The discussion then turns to spherical bodies, where directions are relative to center and circumference. Applying this to the world, if the world is spherical, claiming God is in a direction forces Him to encompass the world or leaves a part of the world without Him. Moreover, because of spherical geometry, what is above for one observer is below for another, making a single literal direction for God impossible. The passage then addresses objections from devotional practices like raising hands to the sky or the Prophet's ascension (mi'raj), noting that these are countered by contrary acts such as placing the head on the ground. The Throne is the qibla of supplication, the earth is the mosque for prayer, and the closest proximity to God is in prostration, as per the verse 'Prostrate and draw near.' Thus, these practices do not imply spatial direction for God.

كرية

قبلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God is literally above the world in a spatial sense because we raise our hands to the sky.
Correction: Direction is a property of finite bodies; the world is spherical, so 'above' is relative; raising hands is a spiritual gesture, not a spatial indication.

Checkpoint

A) God is above the entire world
B) There can be multiple 'aboves' and 'belows', making a literal direction impossible for God
C) The Kaaba is the direction for prayer
D) The world is not spherical

Key Takeaway
Direction is a property of finite bodies; Allah is transcendent and free from spatial location, and worship directions are spiritual, not spatial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you raise your hands in supplication, do you ever catch yourself imagining that Allah is 'up there' somewhere beyond the sky? After studying this lesson, how will you train your heart to transcend that mental image and direct your devotion to the One who is beyond all directions?
?
The lesson contrasts raising hands to the sky with placing the head on the ground in prostration. Which of your own acts of worship are you most likely to attach a spatial location to Allah in your mind? What concrete step will you take to purify your intention from such limiting thoughts?
?
You claim to believe that Allah is not confined to any direction, yet your heart may still feel distant because you picture Him far away. What will you do today to truly feel His nearness, as the verse says: 'Prostrate and draw near'?

The Proof That Accidents in God Entail Change and Contingency
Author presents the proof that if accidents occurred in God, He would change, which is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mutakallimun prove that accidents cannot occur in God's essence because they would entail change, which is a sign of contingency.

Core Idea
If accidents existed in God, He would change; change proves contingency, so God cannot be subject to accidents.

The mutakallimun employ two strategies against those who claim accidents occur in God. The first is a direct proof (burhan) showing that such a claim leads to unacceptable consequences. This lesson unpacks that proof step by step, including the verification of its premises and the definition of change.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it, then read the translation. Let the author's argument flow before you break it down.
2
Identify the two premises
The proof has two main premises: (1) accidents subsisting in God make Him described by them, and (2) being described after not being described constitutes change, which implies contingency. Find these in the text.
3
Understand the definition of change
The author defines change as simply going from one state to another, not necessarily the replacement of one attribute by another. Notice how this definition strengthens the argument.
This addresses a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللمتكلمين عليه طريقان في الكلام؛ أحدهما البرهان، والثاني المناقضة في الإلزام. أما البرهان فنقول: لو قامت الحوادث بذات الباري سبحانه وتعالى لاتصف بها بعد أن لم يتصف، ولو اتصف لتغير والتغير دليل الحدوث؛ إذ لا بد من مغير، وتحقيق المقدمة الأولى أن معنى قيام الأعراض بمحالها كونها أوصافا لها، كالعلم إذا قام بجوهر وصف الجوهر بأنه عالم، وكذلك سائر المعاني والأعراض، فليس ذلك كالوصف بكون الباري تعالى خالقا صانعا على مذهب من لم يفرق بين الخلق والمخلوق، فإن المخلوق لا يقوم بذات الخالق والخلق قائم بذاته تعالى عندكم، فيجب أن يكون وصفاً له. وكذلك يقال: أراد فهو مريد بإرادة، وقال فهو قائل بقول، وإذا تحقق كونه وصفا له بعد أن لم يكن موصوفا به فقد تحقق التغير، والتغير خروج شيء إلى غير ما كان عليه، ولا يشترط فيه بطلان صفة وتجدد صفة، فإنه إذا كان خاليا من صفات ثم اعتراه صفات فقد تغير عما كان عليه، فليس للخصم اعتراض على هذه الطريقة إلا منع الاتصاف أو منع التغير، وقد أثبتناهما، ولكنه وضع لنفسه اصطلاحًا في أحكام
And the mutakallimun have two methods against him in discourse: one is the proof, and the second is contradiction in obligating. As for the proof, we say: If accidents occurred in the essence of the Creator, exalted and high, He would be described by them after not being described, and if He were described, He would change, and change is evidence of contingency, for there must be a changer. The verification of the first premise is that the meaning of the subsistence of accidents in their substrates is that they are attributes of them, like knowledge when it subsists in a substance, the substance is described as knowing; and likewise other meanings and accidents. That is not like the description of the Creator, exalted, as creator and maker according to the school of those who do not distinguish between creation and the created, for the created does not subsist in the essence of the Creator, while creation subsists in His essence, exalted, according to you. So it must be an attribute of Him. And similarly it is said: He willed, so He is willing by a will, and He said, so He is a speaker by a speech. And when it is verified that it is an attribute of Him after He was not described with it, then change is verified. Change is the exit of a thing from what it was upon, and it does not require the annulment of one attribute and the renewal of another, for if He was devoid of attributes then attributes befell Him, He has changed from what He was upon. So the opponent has no objection to this method except to deny the description or to deny the change, and we have established both, but he has set for himself a custom in rulings...
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage presents the first method used by the mutakallimun to refute the claim that accidents occur in God's essence: the proof (burhan). The proof proceeds as follows: If accidents occurred in God's essence, then He would be described by them after not being described. If He were described, He would change. Change is evidence of contingency (huduth), because change requires a changer. The author then verifies the first premise: the meaning of accidents subsisting in a substrate is that they become attributes of that substrate. For example, when knowledge subsists in a substance, we call it 'knowing'. This is different from saying God is 'creator' or 'maker' according to those who do not distinguish between creation and the created—since the created does not subsist in God, but creation (the act) does, and that act becomes an attribute. Similarly, if God wills, He is described as 'willing by a will', and if He speaks, He is 'speaker by a speech'. If this description occurs after He was not described, that constitutes change. Change is defined as a thing going from one state to another; it does not require the disappearance of one attribute and the appearance of another—simply being devoid of attributes then acquiring them is change. The opponent can only object by denying either that the accidents become attributes of God or that change occurs, but the author claims both have been established. The last sentence suggests the opponent's objection is just a terminological evasion.

Key Terms
البرهان
التغير
الحدوث
قيام الأعراض
الاتصاف
مغير
المناقضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Change requires one attribute to cease and another to begin.
Correction: Change is simply going from one state to another; acquiring an attribute after lacking it is change, even without annulment.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, what follows if accidents occur in God's essence?
A) God remains unchanged
B) God is described by them and thus changes, indicating contingency
C) God becomes an accident Himself
D) God's attributes are eternal

Key Takeaway
The proof shows that describing God with accidental attributes leads to attributing change to Him, which implies contingency—an impossibility for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever thought that God's attitude toward you changes when you sin or repent? How does this lesson's proof that God cannot change challenge you to trust His unchangeable mercy and justice?
?
If God is free from change, then His command, promise, and threat are absolute and unchanging. Does your daily life reflect a deep reliance on God's unchanging nature, or do you sometimes act as if God's response depends on your fluctuating state?
?
When you read that change in God would imply contingency, do you feel the weight of affirming God's transcendence? Or have you unconsciously attributed human-like changeability to God in your worship or expectations? What must you realign?

Refuting the Hylomorphic Objection Against God's Power
Refutation of the philosopher's objection that preparedness implies hylomorphic composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosopher's objection that God would have potentiality and actuality (like matter and form), and why the Ash'ari response affirms God's transcendence over such composition.

Core Idea
Potentiality in God is impossible; He is pure actuality without composition.

In this passage, the author presents a philosopher's argument that if God's actions involve preparedness, then He would possess potentiality and actuality—characteristics of hyle (matter) and form. This objection challenges divine simplicity. The author responds by affirming that the Necessary Existence is beyond possibility and non-existence, which are sources of evil. Let's examine the argument and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the philosopher's objection
Read the passage and identify the philosopher's claim: every essence that receives a new attribute must have had preparedness before, implying potentiality and actuality.
Focus on the terms 'preparedness' (isti'dad), 'suitability' (salahiyya), and 'potentiality' (quwwa).
2
Identify the hylomorphic implication
The objection concludes that God would possess hyle (matter) and form if He had preparedness. Note how the philosopher links this to divine attributes.
Hyle and form are material principles, incompatible with God's simplicity.
3
Grasp the refutation
The author's response: The Necessary Existence is transcendent above possibility and non-existence. Preparedness is a privative nature, not applicable to God.
This shows why the objection fails—God's essence does not undergo change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد ألزم عليه الفلسفي إلزاما لا محيص له عنه ، فقال : كل ذات لم يحدث فيها معنى ثم حدث فيها قبل الحدوث استعداد القبول وصلاحيته وقوته، ثم إذا حدث فيها القبول تبدل الاستعداد بالوجود والصلاحية بالحصول، والقوة بالفعل، ويلزم أن يكون في ذاته معنى ما بالقوة، ثم معنى ما بالفعل، وذلك بعينه هو الهيولي والصورة، وقد أثبتوا لله سبحانه وتعالى قبل خلق العالم خصايص الهيولي وهي طبائع عدمية، فإن الاستعداد والصلاحية عدم شيء من إثباته أن يكون شيئا، وواجب الوجود لذاته منزه عن طبيعة الإمكان والعدم اللذان هما منبع الشر.
And the philosopher has compelled him with a compulsion from which there is no escape, and said: Every essence in which no attribute has occurred and then an attribute occurs, prior to occurrence there is the preparedness of acceptance, its suitability and potentiality; then when acceptance occurs, preparedness changes into existence, suitability into attainment, and potentiality into actuality. It would then be necessary that in His essence there is something potential, then something actual, and that is precisely hyle and form. And they have attributed to God, glorified and exalted, before the creation of the world, characteristics of hyle, which are privative natures. For preparedness and suitability are the non-existence of something, and its affirmation is that it is a thing. And the Necessary Existence by essence is transcendent above the nature of possibility and non-existence, which are the source of evil.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents a philosopher's objection (ilzam) against a previous argument, likely about the arising of contingent events. The philosopher argues that if we posit that a thing receives a new attribute, then before that attribute occurs, there must be a state of preparedness (isti'dad), suitability (salahiyya), and potentiality (quwwa). When the attribute occurs, these change to existence, attainment, and actuality. This would imply that the essence in question contains both potentiality and actuality, which is characteristic of hylomorphic composition (matter and form). If this applies to God before creation, then God would have hylomorphic properties—privation and potentiality—which are incompatible with His absolute perfection. The author responds in the last sentence: the Necessary Existence (wajib al-wujud) is transcendent above possibility and non-existence, which are sources of evil. Thus the objection fails because God's essence does not undergo change or potentiality.

Key Terms

قوة (quwwa)
هيولي (hayula)
صورة (sura)
واجب الوجود (wajib al-wujud)
إمكان (imkan)
عدم ('adam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that potentiality in God is possible, meaning He could have unrealized capacities.
Correction: The author affirms that potentiality is impossible for God. He is transcendent above possibility and non-existence, which are characteristic of contingent beings.

Checkpoint
What does the philosopher's objection claim would be attributed to God if His actions involve preparedness?
A) Temporal change
B) Hylomorphic properties (matter and form)
C) Multiple wills
D) Infinite attributes

Key Takeaway
The philosopher's objection fails because God, as Necessary Existence, is free from potentiality and privation, which are characteristic of contingent beings.

The Impossibility of Attributing Letters and Sounds to God
Shahristani proves that attributing letters and sounds to God entails corporeality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing letters and sounds to God's speech necessarily implies that God is a body, which is impossible.

Core Idea
Letters require sound; sound requires collision; collision requires a body — so God's speech cannot be letters.

Shahristani presents a rigorous causal argument showing that if God's speech were composed of letters and sounds, it would force us to attribute physical properties to God. Let's trace his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the dense causal language.
2
Identify the causal chain
The passage argues from letter backwards to body. List each link: letter → sound → collision → motion → body.
Each link is necessary for the previous.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that if God's speech had letters, God would be a body with motion and collision, which is impossible for the transcendent Creator.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسموعة ولا هي معقولة، فإن حقيقة الحرف تقطيع صوت، والصوت بالنسبة إلى الحروف كالجنس بالنسبة إلى النوع والعرض بالنسبة إلى اللون، فإن أثبتوا حروفا هي أصوات مقطعة فلا بد لها من اصطكاكات أجرام حتى يتحقق الصوت، فإن اصطكاك الأجرام كالنوع بالنسبة إلى الحركة، والصوت كالنوع بالنسبة إلى الاصطكاك، ولا بد من حركة حتى يتحقق اصطكاك أو تفكيك، ولا بد من اصطكاك حتى يتحقق الصوت، ولا بد من صوت حتى يتحقق حرف، ولا بد من حرف حتى تتحقق كلمة، ولا بد من كلمة حتى يتحقق قول تام، ولا بد من قول حتى تتحقق قصة وحكاية فيلزم على ذلك أن يكون الباري جسماً متحركاً ذا اصطكاك في أجزاء جسمية، ويتعالى الرب سبحانه عن ذلك علوا كبيرًا.
They are not audible nor intelligible, for the reality of a letter (ḥarf) is the cutting of sound (ṣawt). Sound in relation to letters is like genus to species and accident to color. If they affirm letters that are segmented sounds, then these must involve collisions (iṣṭikākāt) of bodies (ajrām) in order for sound to occur. For the collision of bodies is like a species in relation to motion (ḥaraka), and sound is like a species in relation to collision. Motion is necessary for collision or disjunction to occur, and collision is necessary for sound to occur, and sound is necessary for a letter to occur, and a letter is necessary for a word (kalima) to occur, and a word is necessary for a complete utterance (qawl tāmm) to occur, and a complete utterance is necessary for a story or narration to occur. Consequently, the Creator would have to be a body (jism) with motion, possessing collision in its bodily parts. Exalted is the Lord above that, immensely exalted.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that God's speech consists of audible letters and sounds. The author (al-Shahristani) argues that letters are by definition segmented sounds. Sound, in turn, requires the collision of bodies to exist. Collision presupposes motion, and motion requires a body. Thus, if God's speech were composed of letters and sounds, God would have to be a moving, colliding body—an impossibility for the transcendent Creator. The argument follows a strict causal chain: letter depends on sound, sound depends on collision, collision depends on motion, motion depends on body. Therefore, attributing letters to God implies corporeality, which is rejected.

Key Terms
حرف (ḥarf)
صوت (ṣawt)
اصطكاك (iṣṭikāk)
جسم (jism)
حركة (ḥaraka)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's speech is like human speech, composed of letters and sounds.
Correction: This passage shows that letters and sounds depend on physical processes (collision, motion) that require a body, so attributing them to God implies corporeality, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what physical process is necessary for sound to occur?
A) The cutting of letters
B) The collision of bodies
C) The existence of motion
D) The formation of words

Key Takeaway
God's speech is not composed of letters or sounds because that would require a physical body, which contradicts God's transcendence.

Negating Corporeality and Direction from God
Author concludes refutation of corporeality, direction, and sitting for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari tradition definitively negates corporeality, direction, and literal sitting from God, and how these negations are essential for divine transcendence.

Core Idea
God is not a body, not in any direction, and His sitting is not like creation's.

This passage concludes the refutation of anthropomorphic conceptions of God. The author forcefully rejects the ideas that God is a self-subsistent body, occupies a spatial direction, or literally sits. He then uses a striking analogy to show the futility of trying to reform such misguided doctrines. Read carefully to see how the Ash'ari tradition seals the case for divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic and translation to get the overall meaning. Notice the concise style and the emotional force of the rhetorical questions.
The passage is dense; read it slowly.
2
Identify the three negations
The author negates: corporeality (jismiyya), direction (jiha), and literal sitting (istiwa'). List them and note the specific phrasing.
The negation of direction specifically mentions 'above' (al-fawqiyya).
3
Understand the analogy
Reflect on the analogy of the crooked stick and the foolish doctrine. What does it teach about the possibility of reforming an inherently wrong belief?
The analogy implies that anthropomorphism is fundamentally flawed and cannot be corrected.
4
Appreciate the theological stance
See how this passage affirms tanzih (transcendence) as a non-negotiable principle. The author ends with praise of God, showing reliance on divine guidance.
This conclusion reinforces the importance of negating anthropomorphism in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالجسمية القائم بالنفس، ولا بالجهة الفوقية علاء، ولا بالاستواء استيلاء، وإنما هو إصلاح مذهب لا يقبل الإصلاح، وإبرام معتقد لا يقبل الإبرام والإحكام وكيف استوى الظل والعود أعوج ؟! وكيف استوى المذهب وصاحب المقالة أهوج ؟ ! لولا الله الموفق.
By corporeality, the self-subsistent, and not by the above direction, and not by sitting as domination; rather it is reforming a doctrine that does not accept reform, and tightening a belief that does not accept tightening and firmness. How can the shadow be straight when the stick is crooked? How can the doctrine be straight when the holder of the doctrine is foolish? If not for God, the granter of success.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage concludes the fourth foundation on divine transcendence (tanzih). The author negates that God can be described with corporeality (jismiyya) as a self-subsistent body, or with direction (jiha) as being above, or with sitting (istiwa') as domination. He then declares that attempting to reform a doctrine that does not accept reform is futile, comparing it to trying to make a crooked stick cast a straight shadow. The passage ends with a praise of God who grants success.

Equating Christian Trinity with Philosophical Causality
The author equates Christian Trinity with philosophical causality and refutes both with Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author equates the Christian belief in Father and Son with the philosophers' cause-effect emanation, and how both are refuted by Quranic verses affirming divine transcendence.

Core Idea
The errors of the Trinity and emanation are the same: both compromise God's transcendence.

In this passage, the author draws a striking parallel between two seemingly unrelated doctrines: the Christian Trinity and the philosophical concept of necessary emanation. He argues that both are rooted in the same mistaken assumption—that something proceeds from God in a necessary, eternal way. Through Quranic verses, he refutes both and reasserts the absolute lordship of Allah over all creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Pay attention to the key terms: Father/Son, cause/effect, servant/Lord.
Notice the compact style of the author.
2
Identify the parallel being drawn
The author states that the Christian belief in Father and Son is exactly what the philosophers believed in terms of necessary cause and effect. Mark the phrases that equate them.
3
Reflect on the Quranic argument
The author quotes Quranic verses that condemn ascribing a son to God and emphasize that all beings are servants. Consider how this refutes both the Christian and philosophical views.
The verses are from Surah Maryam (19:88-93).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكثيرًا ما دار في خيالي وخاطري: أن الذي اعتقدوه النصارى من الأب والابن هو بعينه ما قدره الفلاسفة من الموجب والموجب والعلة والمعلول "تكاد السماوات يتفطرن منه وتنشق الأرض وتخر الجبال هذا أن دعوا للرحمن ولدا وما ينبغي للرحمن أن يتخذ ولدا" فهو تعالى لم يوجب ولم يوجب ولم يلد ولم يولد، وإنما نسبة الكل إليه نسبة العبودية إلى الربوبية "إن كل من في السموات والأرض إلا آتي الرحمن عبدا" وهو رب كل شيء ومبدعه، وإله كل موجود وفاطره جل جلاله وتقدست أسماؤه ولا إله غيره والله أعلم.
And frequently it occurred in my imagination and thought: that what the Christians believed of the Father and the Son is exactly what the philosophers conceived of the Efficient Cause (al-mujib), the Caused (al-mujab), the cause and the effect. 'The heavens are almost rent therefrom, and the earth splits asunder, and the mountains fall in ruins, that they ascribe to the Most Merciful a son. And it is not befitting for the Most Merciful to take a son.' So He, exalted, did not cause [emanation] nor was caused, nor did He beget nor was He begotten. Rather, the relation of all things to Him is the relation of servitude to lordship. 'There is none in the heavens and the earth but comes to the Most Merciful as a servant.' And He is the Lord of everything and its Originator, and the God of every existing thing and its Creator. May His majesty be exalted and His names be sanctified, and there is no god besides Him. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a powerful critique equating two apparently different doctrines. The author states that the Christian belief in the Father and the Son is identical to the philosophical conception of the Necessary Being (al-mujib) and the necessary effect (al-mujab), i.e., cause and effect. He then quotes Quranic verses (Maryam 91-92, 94) that condemn attributing a son to God and affirm that all created beings are servants of the Most Merciful. From this, he concludes that Allah neither causes in the sense of emanation (لم يوجب) nor is He caused, and He neither begets nor is begotten. Instead, everything relates to Him as servant to Lord. The passage ends with a doxology affirming Allah's uniqueness and transcendence.

Key Terms

الموجب والموجب
العلة والمعلول
العبودية والربوبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Christian Trinity and philosophical emanation are fundamentally different errors.
Correction: The author insists they are the same error: both posit a necessary, eternal relation between God and something else, compromising divine transcendence.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The crucial point is that God's transcendence precludes any notion of emanation or begetting; all beings are servants, not necessary effects or children.

Refuting False Conceptions of Divine Sonship and Emanation
Refutes Christian and philosophical claims of divine sonship and emanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Christian doctrine of Father and Son is essentially the same as the philosophical concept of necessitator and necessitated, and why both are incompatible with God's absolute oneness and transcendence.

Core Idea
Christian Trinity and philosophical emanation are two sides of the same coin — both deny God's absolute oneness.

The author shares a profound realization: the Christian belief in God having a son is functionally identical to the philosophers' idea of necessary emanation. Both imply a relationship of causation or generation that undermines God's transcendence. This lesson unpacks that claim and shows how the Qur'anic verses and rational arguments uphold tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the translation. Notice the author's rhetorical style and the key comparison he draws.
Pay attention to the phrase 'بعينه ما قدره الفلاسفة'.
2
Identify the two groups being refuted
The author refutes Christians and philosophers. Identify their specific claims: Christians believe in Father and Son; philosophers believe in necessitator and necessitated, cause and effect.
3
Reflect on the Quranic verses
The author quotes Quran 19:90-91 ('The heavens are almost rent...') to show the gravity of attributing a son to God. Consider why this reaction is so severe.
This verse emphasizes the seriousness of shirk.
4
Understand the negation and affirmation
The author negates any begetting or necessitation from God, then affirms the correct relationship: all creation is in servitude to God alone.
The phrase 'نسبة الكل إليه نسبة العبودية إلى الربوبية' is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكثيرًا ما دار في خيالي وخاطري: أن الذي اعتقده النصارى من الأب والابن هو بعينه ما قدره الفلاسفة من الموجب والموجب والعلة والمعلول. تكاد السماوات يتفطرن منه وتنشق الأرض وتخر الجبال هذا أن دعوا للرحمن ولدا وما ينبغي للرحمن أن يتخذ ولدا. فهو تعالى لم يوجب ولم يوجب ولم يلد ولم يولد. وإنما نسبة الكل إليه نسبة العبودية إلى الربوبية. إن كُلُّ مَن فِي السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ إِلَّا وَاتِي الرَّحْمَنِ عَبْدًا. وهو رب كل شيء ومبدعه، وإله كل موجود وفاطره جل جلاله وتقدست أسماؤه ولا إله غيره.
And often passed through my mind and thought: that what the Christians believed of the Father and the Son is exactly what the philosophers assigned of the necessitator and the necessitated, and the cause and the effect. The heavens are almost rent from it, and the earth splits, and the mountains fall in ruins, that they called upon the Most Merciful a son, and it is not befitting for the Most Merciful to take a son. So He, exalted, did not necessitate and was not necessitated, and He did not beget and was not begotten. Rather, the relation of all to Him is the relation of servitude to lordship. There is none in the heavens and the earth except that he comes to the Most Merciful as a servant. And He is the Lord of everything and its Originator, and the God of every existent and its Creator. Glorified is His majesty and sanctified are His names, and there is no god but Him.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a refutation. The author begins by stating a recurrent thought: the Christian doctrine of the Father and Son is identical to the philosophical concept of the Necessitator (al-mujib) and the necessitated (al-mujab), and of cause and effect. He then quotes Quran 19:90-91 to show the gravity of such a claim. Then he affirms God's transcendence: He did not necessitate nor was necessitated, did not beget nor was begotten. Finally, he establishes the correct relation: all creation stands in servitude to God, and God is the Lord, Originator, God, and Creator of everything, alone in His divinity.

Key Terms

الموجب والموجب
العلة والمعلول
العبودية
الربوبية

Correction: God's act of creation is free and voluntary, not a necessary effect, and He is absolutely independent of His creation.

Checkpoint

Philosophers' Objection: Attributes Negate Absolute Self-Sufficiency
Philosophers argue that attributes imply neediness in both attribute and Essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' objection that affirming attributes for God compromises His absolute self-sufficiency.

Core Idea
Attributes imply dependence; absolute self-sufficiency excludes all dependence.

The philosophers, building on Mu'tazilite objections, argue that affirming eternal attributes for the Necessary Existent undermines His absolute self-sufficiency. They claim that any attribute subsisting in God's essence introduces a relationship of dependency between the essence and the attribute, which contradicts God's complete independence. This passage presents their core argument, which the Ash'ari theologian must address.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the philosophers' claim. Note the key terms: 'wājib al-wujūd', 'mustaghni ‘alā al-iṭlāq', and the conclusion about neediness.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the implied conclusion is that the Essence also becomes needy.
2
Identify the premise
The first premise: 'The proof has established that the Necessary Existent is absolutely self-sufficient from every aspect.' Understand what 'absolute self-sufficiency' entails.
3
Analyze the consequence
If an attribute is affirmed as eternal and subsisting in the Essence, then the attribute depends on the Essence for existence, and the Essence depends on the attribute for being qualified. This introduces neediness.
Consider why this violates absolute self-sufficiency.
4
Reflect on the objection's force
Ask yourself: Why is this objection a challenge to the Ash'ari view? How might an Ash'ari respond?
This sets up the Ash'ari reply in the following lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وساعدهم جماعة من الفلاسفة المعطلة في إلزام هذه الكلمات، وزادوا عليهم بأن قالوا: قام الدليل على أن واجب الوجود مستغن على الإطلاق من كل وجه، فمن أثبت له صفة لذاته أزلية معنى وحقيقة قائمة بذاته؛ فقد أبطل الاستغناء المطلق من الصفة والموصوف جميعاً، وأثبت الاحتياج والفقر في الصفة
And a group of the philosophers who are negators (mu‘aṭṭila) assisted them in imposing these words, and added to them by saying: The proof has been established that the Necessary Existent (wājib al-wujūd) is absolutely self-sufficient (mustaghni) from every aspect. So whoever affirms for Him an attribute of His essence, eternal in meaning and reality, subsisting in His Essence, has invalidated absolute self-sufficiency from both the attribute and the qualified, and has established neediness and poverty in the attribute.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage records an additional objection from a group of philosophers (identified as mu‘aṭṭila, those who negate attributes). They support the Mu'tazilite objections and add their own: The proof (dalīl) has established that the Necessary Existent (wājib al-wujūd) is absolutely self-sufficient (mustaghni mutlaqan) from every respect. This is a key philosophical principle: God cannot depend on anything. Therefore, if one affirms for Him an attribute that is eternal in meaning and reality and subsisting in His Essence, then that attribute would be dependent on the Essence for its subsistence (since it subsists in it), and the Essence would be dependent on the attribute for being qualified. Thus, both the attribute and the qualified (the Essence) lose their absolute self-sufficiency. Neediness (iḥtiyāj) and poverty (faqr) are introduced into the attribute (and by extension, the qualified). The objection is that the Ash'ari conception of attributes compromises God's independence. The text ends mid-sentence, likely continuing with the implication for the qualified.

Key Terms

واجب الوجود (wājib al-wujūd)
مستغن على الإطلاق (mustaghni ‘alā al-iṭlāq)
الاستغناء المطلق (al-istighnā’ al-muṭlaq)
الاحتياج (al-iḥtiyāj)
الفقر (al-faqr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'self-sufficient' means God does not need anything external, but the philosophers use it to deny any internal dependence between essence and attributes.
Correction: Absolute self-sufficiency excludes even internal dependency between essence and attributes.

Checkpoint

B) Absolute self-sufficiency.
C) Eternity.
D) Unity.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that affirming eternal attributes for God introduces dependency, violating His absolute self-sufficiency.

How can you shift from a transactional view of worship to one that confesses your complete poverty before Him?

Refuting the Abstraction-to-Knowledge Argument
The author refutes the claim that abstraction from matter entails self-knowledge, showing it is circular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that God's abstraction from matter entails self-knowledge is circular and fails as a valid proof.

Core Idea
Negating limitations does not prove positive attributes like knowledge.

A common rationalist approach attempts to prove God's self-knowledge by arguing that His freedom from matter removes any veil from Himself, so He must know Himself. In this passage, the author exposes the flaw in this reasoning: it assumes what it tries to prove and fails to establish knowledge from mere negation. Let's examine the argument and its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Notice the structure: an opponent's claim followed by a rebuttal.
2
Identify the opponent's argument
The opponents say: God is free from matter (barī’ min al-mādda) → not veiled from Himself → knows Himself. Write this chain in your own words.
3
Understand the critique of circularity
The author says 'this is circular reasoning (muṣādara)'. Explain why 'not veiled from Himself' is the same as 'knows Himself', making the argument assume its conclusion.
This is the key logical flaw.
4
Understand why negation does not imply knowledge
The author points out that negating material properties (like being finite, divisible) does not automatically give the positive attribute of knowledge. Ask yourself: why is that a problem for the opponent?
5
Reflect on the validity of the proof
The author concludes that this argument is not a valid demonstration (burhān). Compare this with what a proper proof would require.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا: طريقنا في ذلك إنما هو برئ من المادة وعلائقها، فغير محتجب عن ذاته؛ لأن الحجاب هو المادة، والمقدس عن المادة هو عالم لنفسه بنفسه إذ لا حجاب. قيل: هذه مصادرة على المطلوب الأول، فإن معنى قولكم: غير محتجب عن ذاته؛ أي هو عالم بذاته، والكلام فيه كالكلام في الأول، وتقريره إنما هو برئ من المادة، فهو عالم فلم قلتم: إنما هو برئ عن المادة عالم؟ ونفي المادة كيف يناسب العالمية والعلم؟ هذا كمن نفى التناهي والانقسام والأين والكيف عنه لم يجب من ذلك أن يكون عالما، فنفي الجسمية والهيولانية عنه ليس يقتضي أن يكون عالما، ولم نجد لعامتكم برهانا على ثبوت كونه عالما بالمعلومات، سواء التجرد عن المادة وعلائقها، وليس ذلك حدا أوسط في برهان أن ولا في برهان لم.
They said: Our way in this is that He is free from matter and its attachments, so He is not veiled from His essence; because the veil is matter, and the one sanctified from matter is knowing of Himself by Himself since there is no veil. It was said: This is circular reasoning (muṣādara) on the first demand. For the meaning of your statement 'He is not veiled from His essence' is that He is knowing of His essence, and the discussion about it is like the discussion about the first. Its formulation is: He is free from matter, so He is knowing. But why do you say: He is free from matter, therefore knowing? And how does the negation of matter correspond to worldliness (al-ʿālamiyya) and knowledge? This is like someone who negates finitude, division, place, and quality from Him – it does not necessarily follow from that that He is knowing. So the negation of corporeality and hylomorphism from Him does not necessitate that He is knowing. And we have not found from your group a proof for the establishment of His being knowing of known things, whether abstraction from matter and its attachments, and that is not a middle term in a demonstration of the fact (burhān anna) nor in a demonstration of the reason (burhān lima).
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The opponents propose that because God is free from matter, He is not veiled from His own essence, thus He necessarily knows Himself. The author responds that this is circular: claiming He is not veiled from Himself is the same as saying He knows Himself. Moreover, the negation of material properties does not imply the positive attribute of knowledge, just as negating finitude does not entail knowledge. Therefore, this reasoning is not a valid proof (neither burhān anna nor burhān lima).

Key Terms
برئ من المادة
حجاب
مصادرة
تجرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Being free from matter automatically means God knows Himself.
Correction: Freedom from matter does not entail knowledge; knowledge is a separate attribute that requires independent proof.

Checkpoint
What logical fallacy does the author identify in the argument that abstraction implies self-knowledge?
A) Begging the question (circular reasoning).
B) False analogy.
C) Hasty generalization.
D) Appeal to authority.

Key Takeaway
The claim that abstraction from matter implies self-knowledge is circular and fails to prove divine knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The author dismissed the argument for lacking both proof of the fact and proof of the reason. Have you ever shared your faith using arguments that sound good but are actually unsound? How would you feel if someone relied on your flawed argument and later abandoned the faith because it collapsed? This passage calls us to be rigorous. What is one weak argument you need to stop using, and what stronger evidence from revelation can replace it?

The Philosophers' Claim and the Challenge of Opposition
Dialectical refutation opening with the philosophers' claim and a dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial dialectical move against the claim that pure evil does not exist, showing that if philosophers admit ordering in existence, they must also admit opposition between good and evil.

Core Idea
If existence has degrees, it must also have opposites like good and evil.

The philosophers argue that existence is entirely good and pure evil does not exist. The author counters by pointing out that if they admit a ranking in existence (some things are more existent than others), they should also admit a polarity where some existents are all good and others all evil. He then invokes scriptural testimonies of angels (all good) and devils (all evil) and presents a dilemma about the source of the world's existents.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophers' claim
Read the first line of the passage where the philosophers assert that existence is all good and pure evil is non-existent.
This is the target of the refutation.
2
Understand the argument from ordering
Notice that the author accuses the philosophers of inconsistency: they admit ordering in existence but deny opposition. If there is a spectrum, why not opposites?
3
Examine the scriptural and philosophical references
The author brings in angels and devils from religious laws, and then references dualists and theologians to pressure the philosophers into a dilemma.
The dilemma: either the world's existents have no support (meaning no creator) or their support is either pure good or pure evil—both problematic.
4
Reflect on the unanswered question
The passage ends with a rhetorical demand for an answer. Consider why this challenge is effective against the philosophical position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منهاج كونها معلومة، وقولكم الشر المحض ليس بموجود أصلا، فإن الوجود اشتمل على الخير) أو هو خير كله.
فنقول: أثبتم ترتيبا في الوجود حتى قضيتم بأن الوجود في بعض الموجودات أول وأولى، وفي بعضها لا أول ولا أولى، فهلا أثبتم فيه تضادًا أيضا حتى تحكموا بأن الوجود في بعض الموجودات خير كله وفي بعضها شر كله، وقد سمعتم من أصحاب الشرائع إثبات الملائكة الروحانيين وذلك خير كله، وإثبات الشياطين وهذا شر كله وأصحاب الأصلين النور والظلمة يقرون عليكم السلام للإلزام وأصحاب الكلام يلزمونكم إثبات موجودات العالم غير مستندة إلى الموجد كأنها وقعت اتفاقا لا قصدًا، أو حدثت فلتة لا إرادة واختيارًا، فإن كانت لا مستند لها فهي مخلوقة لا خالق لها، وإن كان مستندها خيرًا محضا، فكيف يصدر الشر من الخير؟ وإن كان مستندها شراً محضاً فالشر المحض لا وجود له عندكم، فما الجواب؟ وكيف الخروج عن عهدة الخطاب؟
ونقول: نرى العالم الجسماني مملوءاً بالبلايا والمحن والرزايا والفتن، ومشحونا بالآفات والعاهات والطوارق والحسرات ومتموجا بالجهالات وفاسد الاعتقادات، وأكثر الخلق على الأخلاق الذميمة والخصال اللئيمة، واستيلاء القوة الشهوية والغضبية على العقلية، حتى لا تكاد تجد في قرن من القرون إلا واحدًا يقول بالحكمة الإلهية التي هي التشبه بالإله عندكم أو فرقة يسيرة ترتسم بالمراسيم الشرعية التي هي امتثال الأوامر الإلهية عندنا، بل أكثرهم ( صم بكم عمى يعقلون) (البقرة:166)، ( وما وجدنا لأكثرهم من عهد وإن وجدنا أكثرهم لفسقين) (الأعراف:102)، ( وقليل من عبادي الشكور) (سبأ:13)، فكيف يستمر لكم معاشر الفلاسفة أن الشر في العالم بمطلقه لا يوجد وبأكثره لا يتحقق، وقد صادفتم الوجود على خلاف ما قررتم، فاعتبروا النفوس الإنسانية والغالب على أحوالها من العلم والجهل وعلى أخلاقها من الحسن والقبيح، وعلى أقوالها من الصدق والكذب، وعلى أفعالها من الشر والخير تعلمون أن الشر في العالم الجسماني أغلب وأكثر خصوصا في النفوس الإنسانية.
وبالجملة فحيث ما وجدنا الفطرة والتقدير الإلهي غالبا على الاختيار والاكتساب البشري، كان الغالب هو الخير والصلاح، وحيث ما وجدنا الاختيار والاكتساب غالبين كان الغالب هو الشر والفساد، فعاد الأمر إلى أن لا شر في الأفعال الإلهية، فإن وجد فيها شر فبالإضافة إلى شيء دون شيء، وإنما يدخل الشر في الأفعال الإنسانية الاختيارية وهي أيضا من حيث إنها تستند إلى إرادة الباري سبحانه خير، ومن حيث إنها تستند إلى اكتساب العبد تكتسب اسم الشر، غير أن الشرائع قد وردت بإثبات الشياطين وإثبات رأسهم إبليس اللعين، وليس يمكن إنكار ذلك بعد ثبوت الصدق في أقوالهم وأخبارهم بالبينات الواضحة والمعجزات الباهرة، وقد اعترف بوجودها قدماء الحكماء :قالوا : ما من شيء جزئي في العالم إلا ويتحقق له في عالم آخر أمر كلي، فبالحرارة الجزئية استدل على نار كلية.
The method of its being known. And your statement that pure evil does not exist at all, for existence includes good, or it is all good. We say: You have established an ordering in existence so that you judge that existence in some existents is first and more worthy, and in some it is neither first nor more worthy. Why do you not also establish opposition in it so that you judge that existence in some existents is all good and in some is all evil? And you have heard from the people of the laws the affirmation of spiritual angels, and that is all good, and the affirmation of devils, and that is all evil. The followers of the two principles of light and darkness acknowledge you with peace for the sake of refutation, and the theologians oblige you to affirm that the existents of the world are not dependent on the Existent, as if they occurred by chance not by intention, or happened randomly without will and choice. If they have no support, then they are created without a creator. If their support is pure good, how can evil issue from good? And if their support is pure evil, then pure evil has no existence according to you. So what is the answer? And how to escape the responsibility of the speech? And we say: We see the corporeal world filled with calamities, tribulations, misfortunes, and temptations, and packed with afflictions, defects, sudden events, and regrets, and surging with ignorances and corrupt beliefs. Most of creation are upon blameworthy morals and base traits, and the predominance of the appetitive and irascible powers over the rational, to the extent that you hardly find in any century except one who speaks of divine wisdom, which is assimilation to the divine according to you, or a small group that adheres to the religious laws, which are compliance with divine commands according to us. Rather, most of them are deaf, dumb, blind, they do not reason (Quran 2:166). And We did not find for most of them any covenant, and indeed We found most of them defiantly disobedient (Quran 7:102). And few of My servants are grateful (Quran 34:13). So how can you, O philosophers, continue that evil in the world absolutely does not exist and in its majority is not realized, when you have encountered existence contrary to what you determined? So consider the human souls and the predominant state of their condition regarding knowledge and ignorance, and their morals regarding good and bad, and their statements regarding truth and falsehood, and their actions regarding evil and good; you will know that evil in the corporeal world is more prevalent and more, especially in human souls. In summary, wherever we find innate disposition and divine decree predominant over human choice and acquisition, the predominant is good and righteousness; and wherever we find choice and acquisition predominant, the predominant is evil and corruption. So the matter returns to there being no evil in divine acts; if evil is found in them, it is relative to something not something else. Evil only enters into human voluntary actions, and they too are good inasmuch as they are based on the will of the Blessed and Exalted, and inasmuch as they are based on the acquisition of the servant they acquire the name of evil. However, the religious laws have come with the affirmation of devils and the affirmation of their leader Iblis the accursed, and it is not possible to deny that after the verification of truth in their statements and reports with clear proofs and dazzling miracles. The ancient sages acknowledged their existence, saying: There is no particular thing in the world except that there is realized for it in another world a universal command; thus from particular heat they inferred a universal fire.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage is a sustained refutation of the philosophical position that pure evil does not exist. The author begins by quoting the philosophers' claim that existence includes only good or is entirely good. He then counters by pointing out that if the philosophers admit an ordering (tarīb) in existence, they should also admit opposition (taḍād), so that some existents are all good and others all evil. He invokes the religious acknowledgment of angels (all good) and devils (all evil), and notes that the dualists (aṣḥāb al-aṣlayn) also affirm good and evil principles. The author then presents a dilemma: either the world's existents have no support (meaning they are created without a creator) or their support is pure good or pure evil. If the support is pure good, how does evil come from it? If pure evil, then pure evil does not exist according to them. Thus, the philosophers are forced to answer. Next, the author provides empirical evidence from the corporeal world, listing calamities, moral failings, and the rarity of genuine believers, citing Quranic verses to show that evil is prevalent. He concludes with a key distinction: when divine decree and innate disposition dominate, good prevails; when human choice and acquisition dominate, evil prevails. Therefore, there is no evil in God's acts; evil is only in human voluntary actions, which are good in relation to God's will and evil in relation to human acquisition. He also defends the reality of devils and Satan as affirmed by scripture and acknowledged by ancient sages, showing that evil beings exist in the spiritual world.

Key Terms
شر (sharr)
خير (khayr)
وجود (wujūd)
تضاد (taḍād)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What inconsistency does the author point out in the philosophers' position?
A) They deny evil but admit ordering in existence, so they should admit opposition as well.
B) They believe in angels but not devils.
C) They claim existence is all good but also admit pure evil.
D) They reject the idea of a creator.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' claim that pure evil does not exist is inconsistent with their own admission of degrees in existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Empirical Evidence of Evil in the World
Empirical argument listing evils in the world to refute the denial of evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the empirical argument against the philosophers: the corporeal world is filled with evils, moral corruption, and ignorance, contradicting the claim that evil is non-existent.

Core Idea
The world's observable calamities and moral flaws prove the prevalence of evil.

After the initial dialectical challenge, the author now turns to empirical observation. He describes the corporeal world as overflowing with afflictions, bad morals, and corrupt beliefs, citing Quranic verses to show that most people are not righteous. The conclusion is that evil in the physical world is more prevalent, especially in human souls.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of evils
Notice the detailed enumeration of calamities, afflictions, and moral vices that fill the world.
The list is vivid and comprehensive.
2
Consider the Quranic citations
The author quotes verses that describe human ignorance and disobedience, reinforcing the prevalence of evil.
These verses are used as evidence from scripture.
3
Examine the call to consider human souls
The author urges the philosophers to examine human states—knowledge/ignorance, good/bad, truth/falsehood—to see that evil is more common.
4
Understand the conclusion
The passage concludes that evil in the corporeal world is more abundant, especially in human souls.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منهاج كونها معلومة، وقولكم الشر المحض ليس بموجود أصلا، فإن الوجود اشتمل على الخير) أو هو خير كله.
فنقول: أثبتم ترتيبا في الوجود حتى قضيتم بأن الوجود في بعض الموجودات أول وأولى، وفي بعضها لا أول ولا أولى، فهلا أثبتم فيه تضادًا أيضا حتى تحكموا بأن الوجود في بعض الموجودات خير كله وفي بعضها شر كله، وقد سمعتم من أصحاب الشرائع إثبات الملائكة الروحانيين وذلك خير كله، وإثبات الشياطين وهذا شر كله وأصحاب الأصلين النور والظلمة يقرون عليكم السلام للإلزام وأصحاب الكلام يلزمونكم إثبات موجودات العالم غير مستندة إلى الموجد كأنها وقعت اتفاقا لا قصدًا، أو حدثت فلتة لا إرادة واختيارًا، فإن كانت لا مستند لها فهي مخلوقة لا خالق لها، وإن كان مستندها خيرًا محضا، فكيف يصدر الشر من الخير؟ وإن كان مستندها شراً محضاً فالشر المحض لا وجود له عندكم، فما الجواب؟ وكيف الخروج عن عهدة الخطاب؟
ونقول: نرى العالم الجسماني مملوءاً بالبلايا والمحن والرزايا والفتن، ومشحونا بالآفات والعاهات والطوارق والحسرات ومتموجا بالجهالات وفاسد الاعتقادات، وأكثر الخلق على الأخلاق الذميمة والخصال اللئيمة، واستيلاء القوة الشهوية والغضبية على العقلية، حتى لا تكاد تجد في قرن من القرون إلا واحدًا يقول بالحكمة الإلهية التي هي التشبه بالإله عندكم أو فرقة يسيرة ترتسم بالمراسيم الشرعية التي هي امتثال الأوامر الإلهية عندنا، بل أكثرهم ( صم بكم عمى يعقلون) (البقرة:166)، ( وما وجدنا لأكثرهم من عهد وإن وجدنا أكثرهم لفسقين) (الأعراف:102)، ( وقليل من عبادي الشكور) (سبأ:13)، فكيف يستمر لكم معاشر الفلاسفة أن الشر في العالم بمطلقه لا يوجد وبأكثره لا يتحقق، وقد صادفتم الوجود على خلاف ما قررتم، فاعتبروا النفوس الإنسانية والغالب على أحوالها من العلم والجهل وعلى أخلاقها من الحسن والقبيح، وعلى أقوالها من الصدق والكذب، وعلى أفعالها من الشر والخير تعلمون أن الشر في العالم الجسماني أغلب وأكثر خصوصا في النفوس الإنسانية.
وبالجملة فحيث ما وجدنا الفطرة والتقدير الإلهي غالبا على الاختيار والاكتساب البشري، كان الغالب هو الخير والصلاح، وحيث ما وجدنا الاختيار والاكتساب غالبين كان الغالب هو الشر والفساد، فعاد الأمر إلى أن لا شر في الأفعال الإلهية، فإن وجد فيها شر فبالإضافة إلى شيء دون شيء، وإنما يدخل الشر في الأفعال الإنسانية الاختيارية وهي أيضا من حيث إنها تستند إلى إرادة الباري سبحانه خير، ومن حيث إنها تستند إلى اكتساب العبد تكتسب اسم الشر، غير أن الشرائع قد وردت بإثبات الشياطين وإثبات رأسهم إبليس اللعين، وليس يمكن إنكار ذلك بعد ثبوت الصدق في أقوالهم وأخبارهم بالبينات الواضحة والمعجزات الباهرة، وقد اعترف بوجودها قدماء الحكماء :قالوا : ما من شيء جزئي في العالم إلا ويتحقق له في عالم آخر أمر كلي، فبالحرارة الجزئية استدل على نار كلية.
The method of its being known. And your statement that pure evil does not exist at all, for existence includes good, or it is all good. We say: You have established an ordering in existence so that you judge that existence in some existents is first and more worthy, and in some it is neither first nor more worthy. Why do you not also establish opposition in it so that you judge that existence in some existents is all good and in some is all evil? And you have heard from the people of the laws the affirmation of spiritual angels, and that is all good, and the affirmation of devils, and that is all evil. The followers of the two principles of light and darkness acknowledge you with peace for the sake of refutation, and the theologians oblige you to affirm that the existents of the world are not dependent on the Existent, as if they occurred by chance not by intention, or happened randomly without will and choice. If they have no support, then they are created without a creator. If their support is pure good, how can evil issue from good? And if their support is pure evil, then pure evil has no existence according to you. So what is the answer? And how to escape the responsibility of the speech? And we say: We see the corporeal world filled with calamities, tribulations, misfortunes, and temptations, and packed with afflictions, defects, sudden events, and regrets, and surging with ignorances and corrupt beliefs. Most of creation are upon blameworthy morals and base traits, and the predominance of the appetitive and irascible powers over the rational, to the extent that you hardly find in any century except one who speaks of divine wisdom, which is assimilation to the divine according to you, or a small group that adheres to the religious laws, which are compliance with divine commands according to us. Rather, most of them are deaf, dumb, blind, they do not reason (Quran 2:166). And We did not find for most of them any covenant, and indeed We found most of them defiantly disobedient (Quran 7:102). And few of My servants are grateful (Quran 34:13). So how can you, O philosophers, continue that evil in the world absolutely does not exist and in its majority is not realized, when you have encountered existence contrary to what you determined? So consider the human souls and the predominant state of their condition regarding knowledge and ignorance, and their morals regarding good and bad, and their statements regarding truth and falsehood, and their actions regarding evil and good; you will know that evil in the corporeal world is more prevalent and more, especially in human souls. In summary, wherever we find innate disposition and divine decree predominant over human choice and acquisition, the predominant is good and righteousness; and wherever we find choice and acquisition predominant, the predominant is evil and corruption. So the matter returns to there being no evil in divine acts; if evil is found in them, it is relative to something not something else. Evil only enters into human voluntary actions, and they too are good inasmuch as they are based on the will of the Blessed and Exalted, and inasmuch as they are based on the acquisition of the servant they acquire the name of evil. However, the religious laws have come with the affirmation of devils and the affirmation of their leader Iblis the accursed, and it is not possible to deny that after the verification of truth in their statements and reports with clear proofs and dazzling miracles. The ancient sages acknowledged their existence, saying: There is no particular thing in the world except that there is realized for it in another world a universal command; thus from particular heat they inferred a universal fire.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage is a sustained refutation of the philosophical position that pure evil does not exist. The author begins by quoting the philosophers' claim that existence includes only good or is entirely good. He then counters by pointing out that if the philosophers admit an ordering (tarīb) in existence, they should also admit opposition (taḍād), so that some existents are all good and others all evil. He invokes the religious acknowledgment of angels (all good) and devils (all evil), and notes that the dualists (aṣḥāb al-aṣlayn) also affirm good and evil principles. The author then presents a dilemma: either the world's existents have no support (meaning they are created without a creator) or their support is pure good or pure evil. If the support is pure good, how does evil come from it? If pure evil, then pure evil does not exist according to them. Thus, the philosophers are forced to answer. Next, the author provides empirical evidence from the corporeal world, listing calamities, moral failings, and the rarity of genuine believers, citing Quranic verses to show that evil is prevalent. He concludes with a key distinction: when divine decree and innate disposition dominate, good prevails; when human choice and acquisition dominate, evil prevails. Therefore, there is no evil in God's acts; evil is only in human voluntary actions, which are good in relation to God's will and evil in relation to human acquisition. He also defends the reality of devils and Satan as affirmed by scripture and acknowledged by ancient sages, showing that evil beings exist in the spiritual world.

Key Terms
شر (sharr)
خير (khayr)
جهل (jahl)
علم (ʿilm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the author claim about the prevalence of evil in human souls?
A) Evil is rare and exceptional.
B) Evil is more prevalent and abundant.
C) Evil does not exist in souls, only in bodies.
D) Evil is balanced equally with good.

Key Takeaway
The observable world, with its pervasive calamities and moral failures, disproves the claim that evil is non-existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage states that the appetitive and irascible powers dominate the rational in most people. In your own life, which desire or anger has recently led you to disobey Allah? What specific step will you take today to restore the dominance of your rational soul?
?
The author lists ignorance and corrupt beliefs as pervasive evils. Is there a belief you hold that might be corrupt, and why are you afraid to examine it? Will you commit to seeking truth from this lesson and the Quran, even if it costs you comfort?
?
You see evil in the world every day—calamities, moral decay, widespread ingratitude. How does this lesson challenge your tendency to either despair or ignore the problem? What one action will you take this week to be among the 'few of My servants who are grateful'?

Divine Transcendence
intermediate
5 min
The Source of Evil: Divine Decree vs. Human Acquisition
Doctrinal distinction between divine decree and human acquisition as the source of evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological distinction between evil originating from human acquisition rather than from God's direct act, preserving divine goodness while accounting for evil in the world.

Core Idea
Evil comes from human choice, not from God; in relation to God's will, all actions are good.

Now the author provides the key doctrinal resolution. He explains that when divine decree and innate disposition dominate, good prevails; when human choice and acquisition dominate, evil prevails. Therefore, there is no evil in God's acts—evil only enters through human voluntary actions, which are good in themselves as created by God but become evil by human acquisition.

Guided Path
1
Understand the binary principle
Read the first part where the author sets up two scenarios: when divine decree prevails, good dominates; when human choice prevails, evil dominates.
This is the core mechanism.
2
Note the conclusion about divine acts
The author explicitly states there is no evil in divine acts; any apparent evil in them is relative.
3
Examine the attribution of evil to human actions
Evil is only in human voluntary actions, which are good in relation to God's will but evil in relation to human acquisition.
This preserves God's transcendence.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this solves the problem of evil: God is not the author of evil; humans are responsible through their choices.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The belief that God directly creates evil and is responsible for it.
Correction: The author explains that evil arises from human acquisition and choice; God's acts are all good. Evil in human actions is only in relation to human acquisition, not God's creation.

Checkpoint
According to the author, when does evil become dominant in the world?
A) When divine decree is dominant.
B) When human choice and acquisition are dominant.
C) When angels and devils fight.
D) When philosophers deny evil.

Key Takeaway
Evil is not from God but from human acquisition; God's acts are entirely good, and human actions become evil through their own choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you truly believe that the evil comes from your own acquisition and not from God? Can you take full responsibility for your sins without blaming God's decree? How would that change your repentance?
?
Think of a recent good deed you did. Did you attribute it to your own strength or to God's will? According to this passage, that good deed is good because it is based on God's will. How does that make you feel about your own role in doing good?
?
The passage says that when human choice and acquisition prevail, evil results. Look at an area of your life where evil or corruption is present. Is that because your choice and acquisition are dominating over divine decree and innate disposition? What would it mean to allow divine decree and innate disposition to prevail?

Divine Transcendence
beginner

Affirmation of Devils and Spiritual Evil Beings
Final appeal to revelation and ancient philosophy to affirm the reality of evil spirits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the existence of evil beings like devils and Satan is affirmed by religious laws and acknowledged even by ancient philosophers, reinforcing the reality of evil.

Core Idea
Evil spirits are real; they are confirmed by revelation and ancient wisdom.

The author concludes his refutation by appealing to religious authority. He states that the religious laws affirm the existence of devils and their leader Iblis, and this cannot be denied given the clear proofs of prophethood. He also notes that ancient sages acknowledged such beings, using an analogy of particular heat pointing to a universal fire.

Guided Path
1
Read the affirmation from religious laws
Note that the author cites the explicit teachings of the prophets affirming devils and Iblis.
2
Consider the proof from miracles
The truthfulness of prophets is established by clear proofs and miracles, so their statements about evil beings are reliable.
3
Examine the ancient sages' acknowledgment
Even ancient philosophers recognized the existence of universal realities corresponding to particular things, such as a universal fire for particular heat.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
This passage ties together the refutation: evil is real, both in the physical world and in the spiritual realm, as confirmed by revelation and reason.
